And it is said that the correct view is that the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa in this is like theirs. In al-Muhit: The saying of all, and it is said their saying. If the master destroys the sold item in this state, he owes the full value. In al-Tatar Khaniyya: Numbered. And connected to this chapter: If the permitted slave sells some of what is in his hand from trade or buys something with some of the money as trade and makes a concession in that, and that was during the illness of the master, then the master dies of that illness, then according to Abu Hanifa the sale is valid whether he made a concession in the sale to the extent that people overlook such or not, as long as the concession does not exceed one-third of the master's wealth. If it exceeds one-third of the master's wealth, then the buyer has the choice, and if he wishes he can rescind the sale and does not return what exceeds the third, unlike if the master was healthy and the slave made a concession to the extent that people overlook such or that people overlook similar, then it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa however it is, whether the concession exceeded one-third of the wealth or not. And this is unlike the mukatib if he sells or buys and makes a concession during the fatal illness of the master, then it is permissible if it does not exceed one-third of his wealth, so also the slave. All this we have mentioned is the saying of Abu Hanifa. As for according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if he sells and buys to the extent that people overlook such, it is permissible and the buyer takes it, and if he sells and buys with a concession that people do not overlook, then the sale is not permissible according to them, even if the buyer says: I will pay the amount of the concession and not rescind the sale, that is not for him according to them. This is what we mentioned if there is no debt on the slave. As for if there is a debt that encompasses his neck or what is in his hand or does not encompass, then he sells, buys, and makes a concession, small or large, then the answer according to all of them is like the answer in the case where there is no debt on the slave. Al-Faqih Abu Bakr al-Balkhi said: There is no narration from our companions in their books that the small concession from a sick person without debt is counted from his wealth except in this book specifically, so this issue is from the peculiarities of this book. And if the debt is on the master and there is no debt on the slave, then this is two ways: either it encompasses all of the master's wealth or not. If it encompasses his wealth, then the slave sells, buys, and makes a large concession. The issue is subject to dispute: according to Abu Hanifa, the buyer has a choice; according to them, the buyer has no choice. And if the debt is on the master, the permitted one is valid with a small and large concession, and the buyer takes it, so the sale is for all, if the concession does not exceed one-third of his wealth after the debt, and if it exceeds one-third of his wealth after the debt, the buyer has a choice, and the slave's sale is made like the master's sale. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to them, if the concession is small, the sale and purchase are valid and the buyer takes the concession if it does not exceed one-third of his wealth after the debt, and if it does not exceed, he does not take it, and he has a choice. And if the concession is large, the buyer has no choice according to them. And if the master has a debt that encompasses the slave's neck and what is in his hand, and the slave has a large debt that encompasses the slave's neck and what is in his hand, then the concession does not go to the buyer, whether small or large. This is what we mentioned if the permitted one makes a concession. As for if he makes a concession to some of the master's heirs: if he sells to some of the master's heirs and makes a concession and dies of that illness, then the sale is void according to Abu Hanifa, and the heir has no choice. According to them, the sale is valid and the heir has a choice, and it is said: If you wish, you may rescind the sale, and if you wish, you may increase the price to its full value, and nothing of the concession is given to him. In al-Sighnaqi: If the master has a debt that encompasses the slave's neck and what is in his hand and he has no other wealth, then the slave makes a concession during the master's illness, the slave's concession is not valid at all, and it is said to the buyer: If you wish, you may rescind the sale, and if you wish, you may pay all the concession. And if there is no debt on the master, it is permissible. In al-Muhit: The permitted minor slave sold from his father to the extent that people overlook, it is permissible. And if the father sold his property to his son to the extent that people overlook, it is permissible. As for that which people do not overlook, the correct is that it is not permissible. And in it also: If the slave appoints an agent to sell his slave, and he sells it to his master for more than its value, then the master is interdicted over his slave, the agent's acknowledgment of receipt is not believed. And if he sold it to the creditors and acknowledged, he is believed. The difference is that the slave's acknowledgment of receiving the price from his master is not valid because of suspicion, and from the creditors it is valid because there is no suspicion. His saying 'from his master he is believed' applies if he sold to the master's agent or to the master's son who is authorized to buy for the master; apparently the ruling is similar, unlike if the minor buys for himself. I have not seen anyone draw attention to this, and this alert is from the peculiarities of that book. He said generally 'from his master' so it includes whether he is a principal or an agent, and apparently when he is an agent, permissibility without restriction. In al-Muntaqa: If the master buys something from his slave for someone else by agency, the purchase is valid but the receipt is not valid, and if the principal confirms the receipt and the master takes it and it dies in his hand, he is liable for the price to the slave and the sale is void regarding the principal.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on the permitted slave (al-madhun) in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It deals with the rulings concerning a slave who is permitted to trade, specifically when he makes a sale with a concession (muhabah), i.e., selling at a price below market value. The core issue is the protection of creditors' rights and the master's ownership. The text presents the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and applies different scenarios: whether the slave has debts, whether the master has debts or is ill, and whether the buyer is the master or a stranger. The key principle is that the slave's trade is valid, but he cannot harm creditors. If the slave sells to his master at a concession, it is invalid because of suspicion (tuhma) that the master is benefiting unjustly at the expense of creditors. However, if the slave sells to a stranger at a concession, Abu Hanifa allows it if the concession is not excessive, while the two companions permit it only if the concession is small (what people typically overlook). When the master is ill, the slave's concession is treated like the master's own transactions and is limited to one-third of the master's wealth. The text also discusses the minor permitted slave and issues of agency. The author, Imam al-Kasani, synthesizes these rulings from earlier works like al-Kafi, al-Muhit, and al-Tatar Khaniyya. The practical result is that the permitted slave's sale with concession is heavily restricted when it affects creditors or heirs.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-madhun): A slave whom the master has permitted to engage in trade, with the capacity to buy and sell within the scope of that permission.
المحاباة (al-muhabah): A concession in price, i.e., selling at less than the market value, or buying at more than market value.
الغرماء (al-ghurama'): Creditors who have claims against the slave or the master, whose rights are attached to the financial value of the slave's assets.
التهمة (al-tuhma): Suspicion or doubt that a transaction is collusive or intended to harm another's rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a permitted slave sells an item to his master at a price below market value?
A) The sale is valid and the master may keep the item.
B) The sale is invalid because of suspicion of harming creditors.
C) The sale is valid only if the master pays the difference.
D) The sale is valid only if the slave has no debts.

Key Takeaway
A permitted slave cannot sell to his master at a discount; if he does, the sale is invalid because it harms creditors' rights.

Illness and Debts: Extending the Slave's Concession Rules
Expansion of rulings for illness and debts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on a permitted slave's sale with concession when the master is terminally ill (marad al-mawt) or when debts are present, and distinguish the treatment of concessions to heirs.

Core Idea
When the master is sick, the slave's concession is limited to one-third of the master's wealth to protect heirs; debts on the slave or master further restrict validity.

The previous lesson covered the basic dispute on sales to the master. This lesson extends the analysis to complex scenarios: when the master is in his final illness (marad al-mawt), when the slave or master has debts, and when the buyer is an heir. These contexts add layers of restriction aimed at protecting heirs and creditors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Master's Condition
Determine if the master is healthy or terminally ill (marad al-mawt). Also check if there are debts on the master or the slave. These factors change the ruling.
Marad al-mawt triggers the one-third rule from inheritance law, limiting bequests.
2
Apply the One-Third Limit (Marad al-Mawt)
If the master is terminally ill, the slave's concession is valid only if it does not exceed one-third of the master's wealth. If it exceeds, the buyer has the option to either pay the excess or rescind. This mirrors the rule for the master's own gifts in illness.
Abu Hanifa applies the same limit; the Sahibayn also accept it but with nuances on small vs large concessions.
3
Consider Debts on the Slave
If the slave has debts that cover his assets, any concession (even small) that harms creditors is invalid according to all (both Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn). If debts are less than assets, the dispute on size of concession reapplies.
The text clarifies that if the slave's debt encompasses his neck or what is in his hand, all agree the concession is invalid.
4
Consider Debts on the Master
If the master has debts but the slave has none, the ruling depends on whether the master's debt covers all his wealth. If it does, the slave's concession is subject to the one-third limit after debt. The Sahibayn distinguish between small (valid) and large (invalid) concessions.
This is derived from the principle that the master's creditors have priority over the slave's assets that would otherwise go to the master.
5
Special Case: Sale to an Heir
If the slave sells to an heir of the master during the master's illness, Abu Hanifa declares the sale void outright (no choice). The Sahibayn allow it with the heir having the option to pay full value or rescind.
Abu Hanifa extends the suspicion (tuhma) to also protect other heirs from preferential treatment.

Book of the Permitted Slave: It is permissible, and if the debt is two thousand dirhams like its value, his manumission is not permissible. It is not hidden that the execution of manumission according to Imam Abu Hanifa in the case where the debt encompasses his earnings is problematic because the outcome of his madhhab is that he owns the master by way of succession when free, and this one is not free, so it appears that mentioning the neck has no benefit and that what is meant by validity is execution. Al-Sihha: He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And his sale to the master is not valid except for an equivalent value because there is no suspicion in selling at equivalent value, so it is permissible, and with less than it there is suspicion, so it is not permissible, whether the decrease is much or little. And what is meant by non-execution is because of the creditors, for the right of the creditors is attached to the financial value, so he is not allowed to invalidate their right. And it is restricted to the master because if he made a concession to a stranger, according to Imam Abu Hanifa it is permissible because there is no suspicion in it, unlike if a sick person sold an asset to his heir at equivalent value where it is not permissible according to Imam Abu Hanifa because the sick person is prevented from preferring some heirs over others, and with respect to others he is prevented from invalidating the financial value, so he may sell all for equivalent value or less up to two-thirds of the value if there is no debt on him, and this is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions, if he sells to the master it is permissible whether the loss is large or small, but he has the choice between removing the loss or rescinding the sale because in the concession there is an invalidation of the rights of the creditors in the financial value, so they are harmed by it, unlike sale to a stranger with a small loss where it is permissible, unlike if he sells to a stranger with a large concession where it is not permissible at all according to them, and from the master it is permissible and he is ordered to remove the concession, and it is not permissible from the permitted slave according to their principle without the permission of the master, and there is no permission. In al-Kafi: If he sells it to the master at a discount, it is not permissible whether it is large or small, but the master has the choice between removing the loss or rescinding the sale, and this is the saying of some of our shaykhs. And it is said that the correct view is that the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa in this is like theirs. In al-Muhit: The saying of all, and it is said their saying. If the master destroys the sold item in this state, he owes the full value. In al-Tatar Khaniyya: Numbered. And connected to this chapter: If the permitted slave sells some of what is in his hand from trade or buys something with some of the money as trade and makes a concession in that, and that was during the illness of the master, then the master dies of that illness, then according to Abu Hanifa the sale is valid whether he made a concession in the sale to the extent that people overlook such or not, as long as the concession does not exceed one-third of the master's wealth. If it exceeds one-third of the master's wealth, then the buyer has the choice, and if he wishes he can rescind the sale and does not return what exceeds the third, unlike if the master was healthy and the slave made a concession to the extent that people overlook such or that people overlook similar, then it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa however it is, whether the concession exceeded one-third of the wealth or not. And this is unlike the mukatib if he sells or buys and makes a concession during the fatal illness of the master, then it is permissible if it does not exceed one-third of his wealth, so also the slave. All this we have mentioned is the saying of Abu Hanifa. As for according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if he sells and buys to the extent that people overlook such, it is permissible and the buyer takes it, and if he sells and buys with a concession that people do not overlook, then the sale is not permissible according to them, even if the buyer says: I will pay the amount of the concession and not rescind the sale, that is not for him according to them. This is what we mentioned if there is no debt on the slave. As for if there is a debt that encompasses his neck or what is in his hand or does not encompass, then he sells, buys, and makes a concession, small or large, then the answer according to all of them is like the answer in the case where there is no debt on the slave. Al-Faqih Abu Bakr al-Balkhi said: There is no narration from our companions in their books that the small concession from a sick person without debt is counted from his wealth except in this book specifically, so this issue is from the peculiarities of this book. And if the debt is on the master and there is no debt on the slave, then this is two ways: either it encompasses all of the master's wealth or not. If it encompasses his wealth, then the slave sells, buys, and makes a large concession. The issue is subject to dispute: according to Abu Hanifa, the buyer has a choice; according to them, the buyer has no choice. And if the debt is on the master, the permitted one is valid with a small and large concession, and the buyer takes it, so the sale is for all, if the concession does not exceed one-third of his wealth after the debt, and if it exceeds one-third of his wealth after the debt, the buyer has a choice, and the slave's sale is made like the master's sale. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to them, if the concession is small, the sale and purchase are valid and the buyer takes the concession if it does not exceed one-third of his wealth after the debt, and if it does not exceed, he does not take it, and he has a choice. And if the concession is large, the buyer has no choice according to them. And if the master has a debt that encompasses the slave's neck and what is in his hand, and the slave has a large debt that encompasses the slave's neck and what is in his hand, then the concession does not go to the buyer, whether small or large. This is what we mentioned if the permitted one makes a concession. As for if he makes a concession to some of the master's heirs: if he sells to some of the master's heirs and makes a concession and dies of that illness, then the sale is void according to Abu Hanifa, and the heir has no choice. According to them, the sale is valid and the heir has a choice, and it is said: If you wish, you may rescind the sale, and if you wish, you may increase the price to its full value, and nothing of the concession is given to him. In al-Sighnaqi: If the master has a debt that encompasses the slave's neck and what is in his hand and he has no other wealth, then the slave makes a concession during the master's illness, the slave's concession is not valid at all, and it is said to the buyer: If you wish, you may rescind the sale, and if you wish, you may pay all the concession. And if there is no debt on the master, it is permissible. In al-Muhit: The permitted minor slave sold from his father to the extent that people overlook, it is permissible. And if the father sold his property to his son to the extent that people overlook, it is permissible. As for that which people do not overlook, the correct is that it is not permissible. And in it also: If the slave appoints an agent to sell his slave, and he sells it to his master for more than its value, then the master is interdicted over his slave, the agent's acknowledgment of receipt is not believed. And if he sold it to the creditors and acknowledged, he is believed. The difference is that the slave's acknowledgment of receiving the price from his master is not valid because of suspicion, and from the creditors it is valid because there is no suspicion. His saying 'from his master he is believed' applies if he sold to the master's agent or to the master's son who is authorized to buy for the master; apparently the ruling is similar, unlike if the minor buys for himself. I have not seen anyone draw attention to this, and this alert is from the peculiarities of that book. He said generally 'from his master' so it includes whether he is a principal or an agent, and apparently when he is an agent, permissibility without restriction. In al-Muntaqa: If the master buys something from his slave for someone else by agency, the purchase is valid but the receipt is not valid, and if the principal confirms the receipt and the master takes it and it dies in his hand, he is liable for the price to the slave and the sale is void regarding the principal.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on the permitted slave (al-madhun) in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It deals with the rulings concerning a slave who is permitted to trade, specifically when he makes a sale with a concession (muhabah), i.e., selling at a price below market value. The core issue is the protection of creditors' rights and the master's ownership. The text presents the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and applies different scenarios: whether the slave has debts, whether the master has debts or is ill, and whether the buyer is the master or a stranger. The key principle is that the slave's trade is valid, but he cannot harm creditors. If the slave sells to his master at a concession, it is invalid because of suspicion (tuhma) that the master is benefiting unjustly at the expense of creditors. However, if the slave sells to a stranger at a concession, Abu Hanifa allows it if the concession is not excessive, while the two companions permit it only if the concession is small (what people typically overlook). When the master is ill, the slave's concession is treated like the master's own transactions and is limited to one-third of the master's wealth. The text also discusses the minor permitted slave and issues of agency. The author, Imam al-Kasani, synthesizes these rulings from earlier works like al-Kafi, al-Muhit, and al-Tatar Khaniyya. The practical result is that the permitted slave's sale with concession is heavily restricted when it affects creditors or heirs.

Key Terms
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): The illness from which a person dies; a terminal illness that triggers special inheritance rules (one-third limit for bequests).
ورثة (warathah): Heirs of the master, i.e., those who inherit his estate after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on a permitted slave's sale with concession to an heir of the master during the master's fatal illness?
A) The sale is valid but the heir has an option to pay the difference.
B) The sale is void because it favors an heir.
C) The sale is valid if the concession does not exceed one-third of the master's wealth.
D) The sale is valid only if the slave has no debts.

Key Takeaway
During the master's terminal illness, the slave's concession is limited by the one-third rule, and sales to heirs are void under Abu Hanifa.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Buyer's Option When an Authorized Slave Practices Muhabah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on buyer's khiyar in muhabah by authorized slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the buyer's option when an authorized slave makes an excessive muhabah while the master's debt encompasses his wealth, and explain the underlying analogy.

Core Idea
For excessive muhabah by a slave, Abu Hanifa gives the buyer an option; the Sahibayn do not.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a sale involving muhabah (favoring the buyer by undervaluing the price) by an authorized slave when the master is in debt. The key question is whether the buyer has the right to rescind the sale (khiyar) when the muhabah is excessive. Abu Hanifa analogizes the slave to the master, granting the buyer an option, while the Sahibayn treat the slave's act as independent and deny the option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two variations: (1) the master's debt encompasses all his wealth, and (2) the debt does not encompass it. Analyze each separately as the ruling differs.
Focus on the first scenario where the debt encompasses wealth, as the dispute is clearest there.
2
State the rule for excessive muhabah
When the authorized slave makes an excessive muhabah (fahisha) and the master's debt covers all his wealth, note the disagreement: Abu Hanifa gives the buyer an option; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not.
Excessive muhabah is defined as selling at a price significantly below market value.
3
Understand the analogy (qiyas)
Abu Hanifa treats the slave's sale like the master's own sale: if the master had made an excessive muhabah while in debt, the buyer would have an option. The Sahibayn reject this analogy because the slave is not the owner.
The reasoning is based on the slave being an extension of the master for trade purposes.
4
Distinguish slight muhabah
When the muhabah is slight (yasira), the sale is valid and the buyer gets the benefit up to one-third of the wealth after debt. If it exceeds one-third, the buyer gets an option – but this is agreed upon by all? Actually, the Sahibayn also give an option for excessive slight muhabah? Re-read the text carefully.
The text says for slight muhabah, if it does not exceed one-third, the buyer gets the muhabah; if it exceeds, the buyer has an option according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn say if excessive muhabah, no option. So slight/excessive distinction matters.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is whether the buyer can rescind the sale. Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer can walk away if overcharged; under the Sahibayn, the sale stands and the buyer must pay the full price (or keep the item at the undervalued price?).
This affects the buyer's risk and the slave's authority in trade.

أو بما في يده أو لا يحيط فباع واشترى وحابا محاباة يسيرة أو فاحشة، فالجواب فيه عندهم جميعاً كالجواب فيما إذا لم يكن على العبد دين. قال الفقيه أبو بكر البلخي: لا يوجد عن ثلاث أصحابنا رواية في كتبهم أن المحاباة اليسيرة في المريض إذا لم يكن عليه دين تعتبر من ماله إلا في هذا الكتاب خاصة فهذه المسألة من خصائص هذا الكتاب، ولو كان الدين على المولى ولا دين على العبد فهذا على وجهين : إما أن يكون محيطاً مال المولى أو لا بجميع يكون محيطاً ماله. فإن كان محيطاً ماله فباع العبد واشترى وحابا محاباة فاحشة لا يحيط بجميع ماله والمسألة على الخلاف يخير عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما لا يخير المشتري. وإن كان على المولى دين المأذون جائز بالمحاباة اليسيرة والفاحشة ويسلم ذلك المشتري فالبيع من بجميع إن لم تجاوز المحاباة ثلث ماله بعد الدين وإن جاوز ثلث ماله بعد الدين يخير المشتري ويجعل بيع العبد كبيع المولى وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما إن كانت المحاباة يسيرة يجوز البيع والشراء ويسلم المشتري المحاباة إن لم يجاوز ثلث ماله بعد الدين، وإن لم تجاوز لم يسلم ،ويخير وإن كانت المحاباة فاحشة لا يخير المشتري عندهما
Or with what is in his hand or does not encompass, so he sold and bought and practiced muhabah slight or excessive, the answer in it according to all of them is like the answer in the case where there is no debt on the slave. Al-Faqih Abu Bakr al-Balkhi said: There is no narration from three of our companions in their books that slight muhabah in the sick person when there is no debt on him is considered from his wealth except in this book specifically, so this issue is from the particularities of this book. And if the debt is on the master and no debt on the slave, then this is in two ways: either it encompasses the master's wealth or not entirely encompasses his wealth. If it encompasses his wealth, then the slave sold and bought and practiced muhabah excessive that does not encompass all his wealth, and the issue is based on disagreement: the buyer has the option according to Abu Hanifa, while according to them the buyer does not have the option. And if the master has debt, the authorized slave's action is valid with slight and excessive muhabah, and the buyer gets the muhabah, so the sale is from all [his wealth] if the muhabah does not exceed one-third of his wealth after debt, and if it exceeds one-third of his wealth after debt, the buyer has the option, and the slave's sale is treated like the master's sale. This according to Abu Hanifa, while according to them if the muhabah is slight, the sale and purchase are valid and the buyer gets the muhabah if it does not exceed one-third of his wealth after debt, and if it does not exceed, he does not get it and has the option, and if the muhabah is excessive, the buyer does not have the option according to them.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding muhabah (favoring in sale) by an authorized slave. It first presents a ruling from Abu Bakr al-Balkhi noting a unique narration in this book. The main dispute: when the master has debt that encompasses his wealth, Abu Hanifa gives the buyer an option (khiyar) for excessive muhabah, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) deny the option. When the master's debt does not encompass his wealth, the slave's muhabah is treated like the master's: slight muhabah is valid and the buyer gets it up to one-third; if it exceeds, the buyer has an option. For excessive mubahab, again Abu Hanifa gives option, the companions do not. The reasoning ties to the slave being an extension of the master.

Key Terms
محاباة (muḥābāh): Selling below market value; favoring the buyer by charging less than the fair price.
مأذون (ma'dhūn): A slave authorized by his master to engage in trade.
خيار (khiyār): Legal option or right of rescission in a contract.
فاحشة (fāḥishah): Excessive; in this context, a muhabah that is significantly below market value (more than one-third reduction).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, when an authorized slave makes an excessive muhabah and the master's debt encompasses all his wealth, the buyer:
A) Has the option to rescind the sale.
B) Has no option and must accept the sale.
C) Must pay the difference to the market price.
D) The sale is automatically void.

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway: Abu Hanifa gives the buyer an option for excessive muhabah by an authorized slave when the master's debt covers all wealth, while the Sahibayn deny it, reflecting different views on the slave's legal capacity to bind the master's estate.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
8 min
Muhabah with Master's Heir and Sale to Master at Equivalent Price
Intra-Hanafi dispute and zahir al-riwaya ruling on muhabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on muhabah with the master's heir and explain the ruling (zahir al-riwaya) when a master sells to his authorized slave at equivalent value.

Core Idea
Master selling to slave at equivalent value is valid with deferred price; muhabah with heir is disputed between void and valid with option.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the nuanced rulings of muhabah (favorable pricing) by an authorized slave. It first addresses the situation where the slave practices muhabah with the master's heir during the master's death illness, revealing a clear intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. Then it examines the master selling his own property to the slave at a price equal to or less than market value, establishing the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwaya) that such a sale is valid with the price deferred until delivery. Understanding these rulings requires careful attention to the conceptual distinction between the master as a 'stranger' to the slave's earnings and the special scrutiny of transactions with heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main issues
The passage addresses two distinct scenarios: (1) the authorized slave practicing muhabah with the master's heir, and (2) the master selling his own property to the slave at equivalent value. Separate these issues in your mind.
2
Analyze the dispute on muhabah with heir
Focus on the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa voids the sale entirely, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad validate it but give the heir an option to pay full price or rescind. Consider the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
3
Examine the ruling on master-to-slave sale
The passage clearly states the zahir al-riwaya: the sale is valid when the price is equivalent or less. The key reasoning is that the master is a 'stranger' (ajnabi) to the slave's earnings because the slave is in debt, so the transaction is treated as between two separate legal persons.
4
Understand the price deferment
Note that in the valid sale, the price is not immediately due because the master cannot be a creditor to his own slave; instead, the price is deferred until delivery of the sold item. This parallels the rule for options (khiyar).
5
Note additional scenarios and Abu Yusuf's view
The passage also covers cases where the master sells for more than value (option for master), where the master lends money or deposits goods (priority over creditors), and Abu Yusuf's opinion that the master can recover the sold item if unpaid. These are expansions of the core rules.

ولو كان على المولى دين يحيط برقبة العبد وبما في يده وعلى العبد دين كثير يحيط برقبة العبد وبما في يده فالمحاباة لا تسلم المشتري يسيرة كانت أو فاحشة هذا الذي ذكرنا إذا حابا المأذون، فأما إذا حابا بعض ورثة المولى فإن باع من بعض ورثة المولى وحابا وقد مات من مرضه ذلك كان البيع باطلاً عند أبي حنيفة ولا يخير الوارث، وعندهما البيع جائز ويخير الوارث فيقال: إن شئت نقضت البيع، وإن شئت بلغت الثمن تمام قيمته ولا يسلم له شيء من المحاباة. وفي السغناقي : وإن كان على المولى دين يحيط برقبة العبد وبما في يده ولا مال له غيره فحابا في مرض المولى لم تجز محاباة العبد بشيء وقيل للمشتري إن شئت أنقض البيع وإن شئت أدفع المحاباة كلها، وإن لم يكن على المولى دين جاز. وفي المحيط الصبي : المأذون باع من أبيه بما يتغابن فيه جاز ولو باع الأب ماله من ابنه بما يتغابن فيه جاز فأما بما لا يتغابن فيه الصحيح أنه لا يجوز وفيه أيضاً وإذا وكل العبد ببيع عبده فباعه من مولاه بأكثر من قيمته ثم حجر على عبده أقر الوكيل بالقبض لم يصدق، ولو باعه للغرماء وأقر صدق. والفرق أن إقرار العبد بقبض الثمن من مولاه لا لأنه لا للتهمة ومن الغرماء يصح تهمة فيه ا هـ . وقوله من سيده يصدق بما إذا باع لوكيل سيده أو لابن سيده الذي يشتري للسيد فالظاهر أن الحكم كذلك بخلاف ما إذا كان يشتري الصغير لنفسه، ولم أر من نبه على ذلك وهذا التنبيه من خصائص ذلك الكتاب وأطلق في قوله «من سيده» فشمل ما إذا كان أصيلاً أو وكيلاً، والظاهر فيما إذا كان وكيلاً الجواز بغير قيد. قال في المنتقى : ولو اشترى المولى من عبده شيئاً لغيره بوكالة جاز الشراء ولم يجز قبضه وإن صدقه الآمر في القبض فقبضه المولى فمات في يده ضمن الثمن للعبد وبطل البيع على الأمر، بمثل قيمته أو أقل صح ويبطل الثمن لو سلم قبل قبضه وله حبس المبيع بالثمن وصح وكذا شراء رب المال من المضارب عبداً لغيره بوكالة وقيمة العبد ألف ورأس المال ألف يجوز البيع ولم يجز قبضه . فلا سقط عنه . قال رحمه الله : (وإن باع سيده منه بمثل قيمته أو أقل صح لأن المولى أجنبي عن كسب عبده إذا كان عليه دين كما هنا وهذا ظاهر الرواية وهو الصحيح، وقيل هذا بيع لا يجوز لأنه تعذر تجويزه لأنه لا بد للمبيع من ثمن والمولى لا يستوجب ديناً على عبده فصار بيعاً بلا ثمن يجوز. ظاهر الرواية أنه أمكن تجويزه بيعاً من غير ثمن يجب على العبد للحال بل يتأخر إلى وقت تسليم المبيع كما قلنا لو اشترى شيئاً على أنه بالخيار انعقد البيع ويتأخر وجوب الثمن إلى سقوط الخيار، وكذا إذا قبض الثمن ثم سلم المبيع يجب الثمن في ذمة العبد بعد المبيع ثم قيد بقوله «بمثل القيمة أو أقل قال في المحيط : ولو باع من عبده بأكثر من قيمته فالمولى بالخيار إما أن يأخذ مقدار قيمته أو ينقض البيع لأنه رضي بزوال ملكه عن البيع بالمسمى، وإذا لم يسلم له المسمى كان له نقض البيع . قال الشارح: وقوله «يؤمر بإزالة المحاباة يدل على أن البيع يقع جائزاً يجوز أن يكون على الروايتين عند أبي حنيفة على ما بينا. وفي المحيط : ولو أقرض المولى عبده المأذون المديون ألفاً فالمولى أحق بها، وكذلك إن أودعه وديعة فاشترى العقد بها متاعاً فالمولى أحق بالمتاع لأنه بدل ماله والظاهر أن قوله «من سيده» مثال فلو باع وكيل سيده منه كان الحكم كذلك. قال رحمه الله : ( ويبطل الثمن لو سلم قبل قبضه) أي لو سلم المولى المبيع قبل قبض الثمن بطل الثمن فلا يطالب بشيء لأنه بتسليم المبيع سقط حقه في الحبس ولا يجب له على عبده دين فخرج محاباة. وفي الإبانة : ولهذه المسألة زيادة ذكرها في المنتقى فقال : عبد مأذون عليه دين باع المولى منه ثوباً في يد المولى كان الثمن ديناً للمولى على العبد في الثوب يباع الثوب فيستوفي في المولى دينه من ثمنه والفضل للغرماء، وإن كان فيه نقصان بطل ذلك القدر اهـ . بخلاف ما إذا كان الثمن عرضاً حيث يكون المولى أحق به من الغرماء لأنه تعين بالعقد فملكه به عنده وعندهما تعلق بعينه فكان أحق به من الغرماء إذ هو ليس بدين يجب في ذمة العبد، ويجوز أن يكون عين ملكه في يد عبده وهو أحق من الغرماء كما لو غصب العبد شيئاً من ماله أو ودع ماله عند عبده أو قبض المبيع بغير إذن المولى، وبخلاف ما إذا باع العبد من سيده فسلم إليه المبيع قبل قبض الثمن حيث لا يسقط الثمن لأنه يجوز أن يثبت للعبد المأذون المديون دين على مولاه ألا : تری أنه لو استهلك المولى شيئاً من اكتساب عبده المأذون ضمن للعبد، هذا جواب ظاهر الرواية. وعن أبي يوسف أن للمولى أن يسترد المبيع إن كان قائماً في يد العبد ويحبسه حتى يستوفي الثمن إلا أن المولى لم يسقط حقه من العين إلا بشرط أن يسلم له الثمن ولم يسلم فبقي حقه في العين على حاله فيتمكن من استرداده ما بقي العين قائماً في يده لأنه يجوز أن تكون العين
And if the master had debt encompassing the slave's person and what is in his hand, and the slave had much debt encompassing his person and what is in his hand, then the muhabah does not go to the buyer, whether slight or excessive. This is what we mentioned when the authorized slave practices muhabah. As for when he practices muhabah with some of the master's heirs: if he sold to some of the master's heirs and practiced muhabah and died from that illness, the sale is void according to Abu Hanifa and the heir does not have the option, while according to them the sale is valid and the heir has the option: it is said, 'If you wish, you may rescind the sale, and if you wish, you may pay the full price and not get any muhabah.' And in al-Sighnaqi: If the master had debt encompassing the slave's person and what is in his hand and he has no other property, so he practiced muhabah during the master's illness, the slave's muhabah is not valid for anything, and it is said to the buyer: 'If you wish, you may rescind the sale, and if you wish, you may pay all the muhabah.' If the master had no debt, it is valid. And in al-Muhit: The minor authorized slave who sold to his father for a negligible amount is valid, and if the father sold his property to his son for a negligible amount, it is valid. As for what is not negligible, the correct view is that it is not valid. And also: If the slave authorized someone to sell his slave, and he sold him to his master for more than his value, then he was placed under interdiction, and the agent acknowledged receipt, he is not believed. If he sold him to the creditors, and he acknowledged, he is believed. The difference is that the slave's acknowledgment of receipt of the price from his master is not valid due to suspicion, while from the creditors it is valid. His saying 'from his master' is believed when he sold to the master's agent or to the master's son who buys for the master; the apparent ruling is the same, unlike if the child buys for himself. I have not seen anyone point this out, and this notification is a particularity of that book. His saying 'from his master' is general, including whether he is principal or agent, and the apparent ruling regarding an agent is permissibility without restriction. He said in al-Muntaqa: If the master bought something from his slave for someone else by agency, the purchase is valid but the delivery is not valid, and if the principal confirms the delivery and the master takes it, then it perishes in his hand, he is liable for the price to the slave and the sale to the principal is void. A sale for equivalent value or less is valid, and the price is void if delivered before receipt, and he has the right to retain the sold item for the price, and it is valid. Likewise, if the owner of capital buys a slave from the mudarib for someone else by agency, and the slave's value is 1000 and the capital is 1000, the sale is valid but delivery is not valid, so [the obligation] does not drop from him. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if his master sold to him for equivalent value or less, it is valid) because the master is a stranger to his slave's earnings when the slave is in debt, as here. This is the apparent narration and it is correct. It is said: This sale is not valid because it is impossible to validate it, since the sold item requires a price, and the master does not have a debt owed by his slave, so it becomes a sale without a price, which is valid. The apparent narration is that it can be validated as a sale without a price due immediately, but it is deferred until the time of delivery of the sold item, as we said: if someone buys something with the option, the sale is concluded and the obligation of the price is deferred until the option falls. Likewise, if he receives the price then delivers the sold item, the price is owed in the slave's liability after the sale. Then he restricts with his saying 'for equivalent value or less'. He said in al-Muhit: If he sold to his slave for more than its value, the master has the option: either to take an amount equal to its value or to rescind the sale, because he consented to the removal of his ownership by the named price, and if the named price is not delivered to him, he may rescind. The commentator said: His saying 'he is ordered to remove the muhabah' indicates that the sale occurs as valid; it may be on both narrations according to Abu Hanifa as we explained. And in al-Muhit: If the master lent his indebted authorized slave a thousand, the master has priority to it. Likewise, if he deposited a deposit and the slave bought goods with it, the master has priority to the goods because it is the substitute for his property. The apparent is that his saying 'from his master' is an example, so if the master's agent sold to him, the ruling is the same. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the price is void if delivered before receipt) meaning if the master delivered the sold item before receiving the price, the price is void and he does not claim anything, because by delivering the sold item his right of retention falls, and he has no debt on his slave, so it becomes muhabah. And in al-Ibanah: This issue has an addition mentioned in al-Muntaqa: He said: An authorized slave with debt, the master sold him a garment in the master's hand, the price becomes a debt of the master on the slave in the garment; the garment is sold and the master collects his debt from its price, and the surplus goes to the creditors. If there is a shortfall, that amount is void. Unlike if the price is a commodity, the master has priority over the creditors because it is determined by the contract, so he owns it by it according to him, and according to them it is attached to the specific item, so he has priority over the creditors because it is not a debt owed in the slave's liability. It is permissible that the specific property of the master be in his slave's hand, and he has priority over the creditors, as if the slave usurped something from his property or deposited his property with his slave or received the sold item without the master's permission. Unlike if the slave sold to his master and delivered the sold item before receiving the price, where the price does not drop, because it is possible that the authorized indebted slave has a debt against his master. Do you not see that if the master consumed something from the earnings of his authorized slave, he is liable to the slave? This is the answer of the apparent narration. And from Abu Yusuf: The master may recover the sold item if it still exists in the slave's hand and detain it until he receives the price, except that the master did not drop his right to the specific property except on condition that the price be delivered to him, and it was not delivered, so his right to the specific property remains as it was, so he can recover it as long as the specific property remains in his hand, because it is possible that the specific property...
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings. First, when both master and slave have debts encompassing their property, muhabah is not allowed. Second, muhabah with the master's heir: Abu Hanifa voids the sale, while the companions validate it with an option for the heir. Third, the master selling to the slave at equivalent value or less is valid because the master is a stranger to the slave's earnings when the latter has debts; the price is deferred until delivery. This is the relied-upon view (zahir al-riwaya). Fourth, if the master sells for more than value, he has an option. Fifth, if the master lends or deposits money with the slave, the master has priority over creditors. Sixth, if the master delivers the sold item before payment, the price is void. The passage ends with a view from Abu Yusuf allowing the master to recover the item if unpaid. Throughout, the intra-Hanafi disputes are highlighted.

Key Terms
وارث (wārith): Heir of the master; the slave's muhabah with them is treated specially.
ظاهر الرواية (ẓāhir al-riwāyah): The transmitted and well-known opinion from the early authorities (especially Imam Muhammad).
محاباة (muḥābāh): Favorable pricing; selling at less than market value or giving a discount without consideration.
أجنبي (ajnabī): A stranger; in this context, the master is considered a 'stranger' to the slave's earnings when the slave has debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the zahir al-riwaya, what is the ruling when a master sells his property to his authorized slave (who has debts) at a price equal to the market value?
A) The sale is void because the master cannot owe his slave money.
B) The sale is valid and the price is due immediately.
C) The sale is valid but the price is deferred until delivery of the sold item.
D) The sale is valid only if the slave's creditors agree.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a master to sell to his indebted authorized slave at equivalent value, with the price deferred until delivery; muhabah with the master's heir during illness is disputed, with the dominant view giving the heir an option to rectify the favor.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
The Validity of a Master Selling to His Indebted Slave
Explains the validity and conditions of a master selling to his indebted slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of a master selling to his indebted slave, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the rule regarding price deferment.

Core Idea
A master can validly sell to his indebted slave; the price is deferred until delivery.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: can a master sell to his own indebted slave at market value or less? While some narrations claim the sale is impossible because the master cannot own a debt against himself, the authoritative position (Zahir al-Riwayah) upholds validity, drawing an analogy to deferred-price sales like khiyar. Understanding this ruling clarifies the master's rights of retention and the impact of delivery on the price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action is being discussed: a master selling an item to his slave who is permitted to trade and has debts. According to the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah), what is the ruling?
The ruling is sahih (valid) as long as the price is at market value or less.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note that some Hanafi scholars argue the sale is invalid because no valid price can be established (the master cannot have a debt on his slave). Identify the opposing view.
This is a weaker narration; the mufta bihi is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
Examine the reasoning: the master is a 'stranger' (ajnabi) to the slave's earnings because the slave's debts to third parties take priority. The price becomes a debt on the slave after delivery, analogous to a sale with khiyar where the price is deferred until the option is resolved.
This analogy is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) based on the illa of deferral.
4
Explore the effect of delivery before payment
What happens if the master delivers the item without receiving the price first? The price becomes void and the transaction is treated as a gift (muhabat). The master loses the right to claim the price.
Exception: if the price is a specific commodity (not cash), the master retains priority over other creditors.
5
Understand the master's right of retention
The master has the right to retain (habs) the sold item until the price is paid. The sale does not transfer possessory right until the price is received. This right makes the master more entitled to the item than other creditors.
Abu Yusuf added that if the item still exists in the slave's hand, the master can reclaim it even after delivery conditional on payment.

قال رحمه الله : (وإن باع سيده منه بمثل قيمته أو أقل صح لأن المولى أجنبي عن كسب عبده إذا كان عليه دين كما هنا وهذا ظاهر الرواية وهو الصحيح، وقيل هذا بيع لا يجوز لأنه تعذر تجويزه لأنه لا بد للمبيع من ثمن والمولى لا يستوجب ديناً على عبده فصار بيعاً بلا ثمن يجوز. ظاهر الرواية أنه أمكن تجويزه بيعاً من غير ثمن يجب على العبد للحال بل يتأخر إلى وقت تسليم المبيع كما قلنا لو اشترى شيئاً على أنه بالخيار انعقد البيع ويتأخر وجوب الثمن إلى سقوط الخيار، وكذا إذا قبض الثمن ثم سلم المبيع يجب الثمن في ذمة العبد بعد المبيع ثم قيد بقوله «بمثل القيمة أو أقل قال في المحيط : ولو باع من عبده بأكثر من قيمته فالمولى بالخيار إما أن يأخذ مقدار قيمته أو ينقض البيع لأنه رضي بزوال ملكه عن البيع بالمسمى، وإذا لم يسلم له المسمى كان له نقض البيع . قال الشارح: وقوله «يؤمر بإزالة المحاباة يدل على أن البيع يقع جائزاً يجوز أن يكون على الروايتين عند أبي حنيفة على ما بينا. وفي المحيط : ولو أقرض المولى عبده المأذون المديون ألفاً فالمولى أحق بها، وكذلك إن أودعه وديعة فاشترى العقد بها متاعاً فالمولى أحق بالمتاع لأنه بدل ماله والظاهر أن قوله «من سيده» مثال فلو باع وكيل سيده منه كان الحكم كذلك. قال رحمه الله : ( ويبطل الثمن لو سلم قبل قبضه) أي لو سلم المولى المبيع قبل قبض الثمن بطل الثمن فلا يطالب بشيء لأنه بتسليم المبيع سقط حقه في الحبس ولا يجب له على عبده دين فخرج محاباة. وفي الإبانة : ولهذه المسألة زيادة ذكرها في المنتقى فقال : عبد مأذون عليه دين باع المولى منه ثوباً في يد المولى كان الثمن ديناً للمولى على العبد في الثوب يباع الثوب فيستوفي في المولى دينه من ثمنه والفضل للغرماء، وإن كان فيه نقصان بطل ذلك القدر اهـ . بخلاف ما إذا كان الثمن عرضاً حيث يكون المولى أحق به من الغرماء لأنه تعين بالعقد فملكه به عنده وعندهما تعلق بعينه فكان أحق به من الغرماء إذ هو ليس بدين يجب في ذمة العبد، ويجوز أن يكون عين ملكه في يد عبده وهو أحق من الغرماء كما لو غصب العبد شيئاً من ماله أو ودع ماله عند عبده أو قبض المبيع بغير إذن المولى، وبخلاف ما إذا باع العبد من سيده فسلم إليه المبيع قبل قبض الثمن حيث لا يسقط الثمن لأنه يجوز أن يثبت للعبد المأذون المديون دين على مولاه ألا : تری أنه لو استهلك المولى شيئاً من اكتساب عبده المأذون ضمن للعبد، هذا جواب ظاهر الرواية. وعن أبي يوسف أن للمولى أن يسترد المبيع إن كان قائماً في يد العبد ويحبسه حتى يستوفي الثمن إلا أن المولى لم يسقط حقه من العين إلا بشرط أن يسلم له الثمن ولم يسلم فبقي حقه في العين على حاله فيتمكن من استرداده ما بقي العين قائماً في يده لأنه يجوز أن تكون العين المملوكة للمولى في يد عبده فكذا يجوز أن يكون له ملك اليد فيه وأما بعد الاستهلاك فقد صار ديناً فلا يمكن ايجابه من عبده ا هـ . قال رحمه الله : (وله حبس المبيع بالثمن) أي للمولى حبس المبيع حتى يستوفي الثمن من العبد لأن البيع لا يزيل ملك اليد ما لم يصل إليه الثمن فيبقى ملك اليد على ما كان عليه حتى يستوفي الثمن، ولهذا كان أخص به من سائر الغرماء، ولأن للدين تعلقاً بالعين لأنه يقابله ويسلم بسلامتها فكان له شبهة بالعين المقابل له فيكون للمولى حق فيه لتعلق حقه بالعين ولهذا يستوجب بدل الكتابة على المكاتب لما أنه مقابل أنه لا يجب له على عبده دين بخلاف ما إذا سلم المولى المبيع أولاً حيث يسقط دينه مع لذهاب تعلق حقه بالعين فيصير الثمن ديناً مطلقاً فيسقط ا هـ .
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (If his master sells to him for like its value or less, it is valid,) because the master is a stranger to his slave's earnings when the slave has a debt, as here. This is the apparent transmission and it is the correct view. And it is said: this sale is not permissible because it cannot be validated; for the sold item must have a price, and the master does not owe a debt to his slave, so it becomes a sale without a price. The apparent transmission is that it can be validated as a sale without a price immediately due on the slave, but rather it is deferred until the time of delivery, as we said: if one buys something with the option of cancellation, the sale is concluded and the obligation of the price is deferred until the option falls. Similarly, if he receives the price and then delivers the sold item, the price becomes due on the slave after the sale. Then he restricted it by saying "for like its value or less." He said in al-Muhit: If he sells to his slave for more than its value, then the master has the option: either he takes the amount of its value or he annuls the sale, because he consented to the removal of his ownership by the named price, and if the named price is not fulfilled for him, he has the right to annul the sale. The commentator said: His saying "he is ordered to remove the favor" indicates that the sale occurs as permissible, and it could be on both narrations according to Abu Hanifa as we explained. And in al-Muhit: If the master lends his indebted permitted slave a thousand, the master is more entitled to it; similarly, if he deposits with him a deposit and the slave buys goods with it, the master is more entitled to the goods because it is the substitute for his property. And it appears that his saying "from his master" is an example; so if the agent of his master sells to him, the ruling is the same. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And the price becomes void if delivered before its receipt,) i.e., if the master delivers the sold item before receiving the price, the price becomes void, so he cannot claim anything, because by delivering the sold item his right of retention falls, and he does not have a debt due on his slave, so it becomes a favor. And in al-Ibanah: There is an addition to this issue mentioned in al-Muntaqa: a permitted slave with debt, if his master sells him a garment that is in the master's hand, the price becomes a debt for the master against the slave in the garment; the garment is sold and the master collects his debt from its price, and the surplus is for the creditors, and if there is a deficiency, that amount becomes void. Unlike when the price is a commodity, where the master is more entitled to it than the creditors because it is specified by the contract and he owns it by it according to him; and according to them, it is attached to its essence, so he is more entitled to it than the creditors since it is not a debt due on the slave, and it is possible that it is an owned property of the master in his slave's hand, and he is more entitled to it than the creditors, as if the slave usurped something of his wealth or deposited his wealth with his slave, or received the sold item without the master's permission. And unlike if the slave sells to his master and delivers the sold item to him before receiving the price, where the price does not become void, because it is possible for the permitted indebted slave to have a debt against his master; do you not see that if the master destroys something of his permitted slave's earnings, he is liable to the slave? This is the answer of the apparent transmission. And from Abu Yusuf: the master may reclaim the sold item if it is still existing in the slave's hand and retain it until he receives the price, because the master only relinquished his right in the property on condition that the price is delivered to him, and it was not delivered, so his right in the property remains as it was, so he can reclaim it as long as the property remains existing in his hand, because it is possible for the master's owned property to be in his slave's hand, so similarly it is possible for him to have possessory right over it. But after destruction, it becomes a debt, so it cannot be imposed on his slave. End. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And he has the right to retain the sold item for the price,) i.e., the master has the right to retain the sold item until he receives the price from the slave, because the sale does not remove possessory right until the price reaches him, so possessory right remains as it was until he receives the price, and therefore he is more specific to it than all other creditors, and because the debt is attached to the property since it is in exchange for it and is safe with its safety, so it has a semblance of the property in exchange for it, so the master has a right in it due to the attachment of his right to the property, and therefore the substitute for kitaba is owed on the mukatib because it is in exchange, unlike when the master delivers the sold item first, where his debt drops because the attachment of his right to the property goes away, so the price becomes an absolute debt and thus drops. End.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale between a master and his indebted permitted slave (ma'dhun). The ruling from the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that such a sale is valid when the price is at market value or less. The reason (illa) given is that the master is a stranger to the slave's earnings because the slave's debts to other creditors take priority. The opposing view, attributed to some scholars, argues that the sale is invalid because no actual price can be established (since the master cannot owe a debt to himself). The commentator defends the Zahir al-Riwayah position by drawing an analogy to a sale with an option of cancellation (khiyar): just as the price is deferred until the option falls, here the price is deferred until delivery. Once delivered, the price becomes a debt on the slave. If the master delivers the item before receiving the price, the price is voided, as it becomes a gift (muhabat). However, if the price is a specific commodity (not cash), the master retains priority over other creditors because it remains his specific property. Abu Yusuf added that the master can reclaim the item if it still exists in the slave's hand, since his relinquishment was conditional on payment. The master also has the right to retain (habs) the sold item until payment, as the sale does not transfer possessory right until the price is received.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade, often with debts to third parties.
محاباة (muhabat): Favoritism; here, if the master delivers the item without receiving the price, it becomes a gift and the price is void.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent transmission from Imam Muhammad's six core books, considered the most authoritative within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Zahir al-Riwayah, what happens if a master sells an item to his indebted slave for market value but delivers the item before receiving payment?
A) The master can still demand the full price.
B) The price becomes void and the transaction is a gift.
C) The sale is invalid and the item must be returned.
D) The slave must pay half the price immediately.

Key Takeaway
The Zahir al-Riwayah position allows a master to validly sell to his indebted slave at market value, with the price deferred until delivery; early delivery voids the price.

Liability for Emancipating Indebted Slave
Explains ruling on master's emancipation of indebted slave and resulting liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a master emancipating his indebted permitted slave and determine the amount of compensation due to creditors.

Core Idea
Emancipating an indebted slave is valid but the master must compensate creditors up to the slave's value.

This passage addresses a situation where a master frees his slave who is deeply in debt. While the emancipation itself is valid by consensus, the creditors' right to recover their money through the slave's person or earnings is jeopardized. Consequently, the master becomes liable to compensate them. Understanding this ruling requires analyzing the interplay between ownership, creditors' rights, and the unique legal nature of emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its validity
The act is the emancipation (i'taq) of a permitted (ma'dhun) slave who is totally covered by debt. The ruling: it is valid (sahih) by consensus (ijma). The basis is that the master's ownership remains intact.
The text explicitly states 'وهذا بالإجماع' (this is by consensus).
2
Understand the creditors' right and the master's liability
Although emancipation is valid, the creditors have a right over the slave's person (raqabah) for debt recovery. By freeing the slave, the master extinguishes that right, analogized to destroying someone else's property. Therefore, the master must guarantee the slave's value to the creditors.
The author uses qiyas (analogy) to destruction of property belonging to others.
3
Calculate the amount of compensation
If the debt is less than the slave's market value, the master pays only the debt amount. If the debt exceeds the value, the master pays the full value, not the debt. This is because the creditors' right attached to the slave's monetary value, not to the debt itself.
The text: 'وإن كان الدين أقل من القيمة ضمن قدر الدين لا غير، وإن كان الدين أكثر من القيمة ضمن قيمته بالغة ما بلغت'.
4
Distinguish from related cases
The rule does not apply if the slave is mudabbar (deferred emancipation) or umm walad (mother of master's child), because creditors do not have the right to sell such slaves for debt. Also, knowledge of the debt is irrelevant, unlike cases of a slave who committed a crime.
These distinctions show the precise scope of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وصح إعتاقه) أي جاز إعتاق المولى عبده المأذون المديون المستغرق قال بالدين وهذا بالإجماع لقيام ملكه فيه وإنما الخلاف في أكسابه بعد الاستغرق بالدين وقد تقدم بيانه. وفي المحيط : ولو دبر المأذون المديون صح فإن شاء الغرماء ضمنوا المولى قيمته ولا سبيل لهم على العبد حتى يعتق فإذا أعتق فلهم أن يبيعوه بما بقي من دينهم رهن عبده المأذون المديون أو أجره وعليه دين إلى أجل جاز، وإذا حل الأجل ضمنوا المولى قيمته في الرهن دون الإجارة، فإن بقيت منها مدة فلهم أن يفسخوا الإجارة لأنها تفسخ بالأعذار بخلاف الرهن. ولو باعه المولى ثم اشتراه أو استقاله ثم حل الأجل لم يكن لهم على العبد سبيل وضمنوا المولى قيمته إلا أن يرده عليه بعيب بقضاء القاضي أو بخيار لأن حقهم قد بطل عن رقبته بالبيع وبرئ العبد عن الدين والبيع بالتراضي بيع جديد في حق ثالث . قال رحمه الله : (وضمن قيمته لغرمائه يعني المولى يضمن قيمة المعتق لغرمائه لأنه أتلف ما تعلق به حقهم بيعاً واستيفاء من ثمنه ولا وجه لرد العتق لأنه لا يقبل الفسخ فأوجب الضمان دفعاً لضرر الغرماء بخلاف ما إذا كان المأذون له مدبراً أو أم ولد حيث لا يجب الضمان بإعتاقهما لأن حق الغرماء لم يتعلق برقبتهما استيفاء بالبيع فلم يكن المولى متلفاً حقهم فلم يضمن شيئاً، فلو قال «ولو قناً» لكان أولى . وإن كان الدين أقل من القيمة ضمن قدر الدين لا غير، وإن كان الدين أكثر من القيمة ضمن قيمته بالغة ما بلغت لتعلق حقهم بماليته كما إذا أعتق الراهن والمرهون بخلاف ضمان الجناية على العبد بحيث لا يبلغ به دية الحر لأن القيمة هناك بدل الآدمي من وجه فلا يبلغ به دية الحر، وكذا لا يختلف بين ما إذا علم المولى بالدين أو لم يعلم بمنزلة إتلاف الغير لما تعلق به حقهم، وبخلاف إعتاق العبد الجاني حيث يجب على المولى جميع الأرش إن كان إعتاقه بعد علمه بالجناية لأنه الواجب فيها على المولى وهو يخير بين الدفع والفداء فيكون مختاراً للفداء بالإعتاق عالماً أو لا كذلك هنا لأن المولى ليس له شيء وإنما يضمن باعتبار تفويت حقهم كإتلاف مال الغير وذلك لا يختلف بين العلم وعدمه، وكذلك
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And valid is his emancipation,) i.e., it is permissible for the master to emancipate his permitted, indebted slave who is totally covered by debt. He said: by debt, and this is by consensus because his ownership remains in him; the only disagreement is regarding his earnings after being totally covered by debt, and its clarification has preceded. And in al-Muhit: If he makes a tadbir of the indebted permitted slave, it is valid; if the creditors wish, they can make the master guarantee his value, and they have no recourse against the slave until he is freed; when he is freed, they can sell him for what remains of their debt. If the indebted permitted slave pawns (rahn) or rents (ijarah) with a deferred debt, it is permissible; when the term ends, they make the master guarantee his value in rahn but not in ijarah; if some time remains in the ijarah, they can cancel it because ijarah is cancellable due to excuses, unlike rahn. If the master sells him and then buys him back or is released (istiqalah) and then the term ends, they have no recourse against the slave and they make the master guarantee his value, unless he returns him due to a defect by a judge's ruling or by option, because their right over his person (raqabah) was invalidated by the sale and the slave is cleared of debt, and a consensual sale is a new sale regarding a third party. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And he guarantees his value to his creditors,) meaning the master guarantees the value of the freed slave to his creditors because he destroyed what was attached to their right, sale and collection from its price, and there is no way to nullify emancipation because it does not accept revocation, so liability is imposed to prevent harm to the creditors. Unlike if the permitted slave is mudabbar or umm walad, where no guarantee is necessary for their emancipation because the creditors' right was not attached to their persons for collection by sale, so the master did not destroy their right and owes nothing. If he had said "even if he is a qinn (ordinary slave)," it would have been better. If the debt is less than the value, he guarantees only the amount of the debt; if the debt is more than the value, he guarantees the full value, whatever it may be, because their right is attached to its monetary value, as when the pawnor frees the pawned slave. Unlike the guarantee for a crime on a slave that does not reach the blood money of a free person, because here the value is in some respects a substitute for a human being, so it does not reach the blood money of a free person. Similarly, it does not differ whether the master knew of the debt or not; it is like destroying someone else's property because the right was attached to it. Unlike emancipating a slave who committed a crime (jani), where the master must pay all the restitution (arsh) if he emancipates after knowing of the crime, because that is the obligation on the master and he has the choice between handing over or ransoming; so by emancipating he chooses ransoming, whether knowledgeable or not. Not so here, because the master has nothing; he merely guarantees due to destroying their right, like destroying someone else's property, and that does not differ between knowledge and ignorance. And similarly.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on a master emancipating his indebted permitted slave (ma'dhun). The central ruling is that such emancipation is valid by consensus (ijma), despite the slave being totally covered by debt, because the master's ownership remains intact. However, since the creditors have a right over the slave's person (raqabah) for the satisfaction of their debts, the master becomes liable to compensate them up to the slave's market value. This is analogous to destroying property that is subject to a pledge or lien. The amount of liability is capped at the slave's value, but if the debt is smaller, only that amount is owed. Knowledge of the debt is irrelevant, similar to destroying someone else's property. The passage also distinguishes cases of tadbir (postponed emancipation) and ijarah, and discusses scenarios where the master sells the slave and then reacquires him, which cuts off creditors' rights over the slave.

Key Terms
إعتاق (i'taq): Emancipation of a slave by the master; here it is valid even if the slave is indebted, but triggers liability.
غرماء (ghurama): Creditors of the indebted slave; they have a right over the slave's person and earnings.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose emancipation is deferred to the master's death; here distinguished because creditors' right is weaker.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master emancipates his indebted ma'dhun slave whose market value is 1000 dirhams, but the slave's debts total 1500 dirhams, how much must the master pay to creditors?
A) 500 dirhams
B) 1000 dirhams
C) 1500 dirhams
D) Nothing, because emancipation is valid by consensus

Key Takeaway
The master's emancipation of an indebted slave is valid but triggers liability to compensate creditors up to the slave's value, because the master destroys their right over the slave's person.

Master's Right to Detain the Sold Item Until Payment (Habs al-Mabi' bi al-Thaman)
Explains the master's right to detain the sold item with talil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the seller's right to detain the sold item until payment, explain the legal reasoning based on milk al-yad, and apply the rule to cases involving creditor priority.

Core Idea
Until the price is paid, the seller retains possessory ownership, giving him priority over other creditors.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi principle: the seller's right to retain the sold item until the price is received. This right is grounded in the concept of milk al-yad (possessory ownership) and has significant implications for the seller's priority among creditors. The source text from Kanz al-Daqaiq, with commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq, provides the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Determine the specific legal right: the master has the right to detain the sold item until the price is paid. Note that this is a permissible right (mubah), not an obligation.
2
Understand the Legal Basis (Talil)
Recognize that the sale does not transfer milk al-yad (possessory ownership) from the master until the price is received. Until then, the master retains his prior possessory right.
Milk al-yad is distinct from full ownership (milk al-raqaba).
3
Recognize the Priority over Other Creditors
Because the debt is attached to the specific asset (the sold item), the master has priority over other creditors. This attachment gives the master a form of security interest.
The commentary compares this to the case of kitaba (manumission contract).
4
Note the Exception: Handing Over First
If the master voluntarily delivers the sold item before receiving payment, the attachment of the debt to the asset is severed. The debt becomes a simple unsecured claim, and the priority is lost.
This highlights the conditional nature of the right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وله حبس المبيع بالثمن) أي للمولى حبس المبيع حتى يستوفي الثمن من العبد لأن البيع لا يزيل ملك اليد ما لم يصل إليه الثمن فيبقى ملك اليد على ما كان عليه حتى يستوفي الثمن، ولهذا كان أخص به من سائر الغرماء، ولأن للدين تعلقاً بالعين لأنه يقابله ويسلم بسلامتها فكان له شبهة بالعين المقابل له فيكون للمولى حق فيه لتعلق حقه بالعين ولهذا يستوجب بدل الكتابة على المكاتب لما أنه مقابل أنه لا يجب له على عبده دين بخلاف ما إذا سلم المولى المبيع أولاً حيث يسقط دينه مع لذهاب تعلق حقه بالعين فيصير الثمن ديناً مطلقاً فيسقط ا هـ .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And he has the right to detain the sold item for the price) meaning the master has the right to detain the sold item until he receives the price from the slave, because the sale does not remove the possessory ownership as long as the price has not been received, so possessory ownership remains as it was until he receives the price. For this reason, he is more entitled to it than other creditors. And because debt has a connection to the asset since it corresponds to it and the asset is safe with its safety, so it has a semblance to the corresponding asset, thus the master has a right in it due to the attachment of his right to the asset. For this reason, he deserves the substitute of kitaba from the mukatib because it is in exchange, as no debt is owed to him from his slave. Unlike if the master hands over the sold item first, where his debt is dropped due to the removal of his right's attachment to the asset, so the price becomes an absolute debt and then it falls.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with reasoning. It addresses the master's right to detain the sold item (the authorized slave) until the price is paid. The commentary explains that the sale does not transfer possessory ownership until the price is received, so the master retains his prior possessory right. This gives him priority over other creditors because his right is attached to the asset itself, like a security interest. The reasoning is based on the principle that debt can be connected to the asset. The passage distinguishes the case where the master hands over the item first, which severs the attachment and makes the debt a simple claim.

Key Terms
حبس المبيع بالثمن (habs al-mabi' bi al-thaman): The right of the seller (master) to retain possession of the sold item until the buyer (slave) pays the price.
ملك اليد (milk al-yad): Possessory ownership or the right of possession that is not necessarily full ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the master have priority over other creditors regarding the sold slave?
A) Because the master is the owner of the slave.
B) Because the debt is attached to the asset and the master retains possessory ownership until payment.
C) Because the master has a written contract.
D) Because the slave is still in the master's possession.

Key Takeaway
The seller's right to detain the sold item until payment is a Hanafi rule rooted in the concept of milk al-yad, giving the seller priority over other creditors.
