Core Ruling: Creditors' Right to Reject Sale of Ma'dhun
Ruling on creditors' right to reject sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which creditors may reject the sale of a ma'dhun slave and identify the effect of informing the purchaser of the debt.

Core Idea
If the master sells the ma'dhun knowing of the debt, creditors can reject the sale to protect their right to the slave's earnings.

This lesson focuses on the primary ruling: when a master sells his ma'dhun slave after informing the buyer of the slave's debts, the creditors have the right to reject the sale. We will examine the conditions that trigger this right and understand why informing the buyer does not bar the creditors' claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the master's sale of the ma'dhun slave with disclosure of the debt. The ruling is that the creditors have the right to reject (radd) the sale.
The sale is valid between the parties but is suspended (mawquf) with respect to the creditors.
2
Determine the conditions for creditors' rejection
Three conditions are required: (1) the debt is due (hal), (2) the sale is not at the creditors' request, and (3) the sale price does not cover the debts.
If any condition is missing, the creditors lose the right to reject.
3
Understand the effect of informing the purchaser
Informing the purchaser drops the buyer's option to return the slave for the defect of debt. This makes the sale binding between the master and the buyer, but still subject to creditors' rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن باعه سيده وأعلم بالدين فللغرماء رد البيع) لأن حقهم تعلق به وهو حق الاستسعاء والاستيفاء من رقبته وفي كل منهما فائدة فالأول تام مؤخر والثاني ناقص معجل وبالبيع تفوت هذه الخيرة وكان لهم رده وفائدة الإعلام بالدين سقوط خيار المشتري في الرد بعيب الدين حتى يلزم البيع في حق المتعاقدين وإن لم يكن لازماً في حق الغرماء. هذا إذا كان الدين حالاً وكان البيع من غير طلب الغرماء والثمن لا يوفي بديونهم، وإن كان دينهم مؤجلاً فالبيع جائز لأنه باع ملكه وهو قادر على تسليمه ولم يتعلق به حق لغيره لأن حق الغرماء يتأخر بخلاف الحال. وفي النهاية : زاد أو رضي الغرماء بالبيع فلا يكون لهم الرد، وهذا بخلاف الرهن بالدين المؤجل حيث لا يجوز له أن يبيعه لأن المرتهن ملك الرقبة فلا يقدر على تسليمه ولا يد للغرماء في العبد المأذون ولا في كسبه، وإذا لم يوجد شيء مما ذكرنا من تأجيل الثمن وطلب الغرماء وفاء الثمن بالدين فالبيع موقوف حتى يجوز بإجازة مسألة الكتاب على ما بينا . لا وذكر محمد في الأصل أنه باطل واختلفوا في الغرماء وهي معناه ؛ فقال بعضهم معناء إنه سيبطل لأن للغير حق ابطاله وقال بعضهم معناه إنه فاسد : إنه إذا أعتقه المشتري بعد القبض أو دبره صح ذلك ويلزمه قيمته . الأصل : و وفي العناية : فإن قيل : إذا باع المولى عبده الجاني بعد العلم بالجناية كان مختاراً الفداء فما باله ههنا لا يكون مختار القضاء الدين من ماله؟ الجواب بأن موجب الجناية الدفع على المولى فإذا تعذر عليه بالبيع طولب به لبقاء الواجب عليه، وأما الدين فهو واجب في ذمة العبد بحيث لا يسقط عنه بالبيع والاعتاق حتى يؤخذ به بعد العتق فلما كان كذلك كان البيع من المولى بمنزلة أن يقال أنا أقضي دينه وذلك عدة بالتبرع فلا يلزمه وفيه نظر لأن قوله أنا أقضى دينه ويحتمل الكفالة فلا يتعين عدة والجواب أن العدة أدنى الاحتمالين فيثبت به لأنه لا دليل على غيره، وإذا جنى العبد المأذون طولب المولى بالدفع أو الفداء لأن الخصم في رقبة العبد المأذون هو المولى لأنها كسب المولى لا كسب المأذون ، ولهذا لو ادعى إنسان في رقبته حقاً . المولى خصماً للمدعي لا للمأذون، وكذا لاتباع رقبة المأذون المديون إلا بحضرة المولى لعله يختار الفداء لأن الإذن للعبد لا يعجز الملك عن الدفع لأنه باقٍ على ملكه، وكل تصرف أصابه المالك في العبد فلا يعجزه عن الدفع لا يصير به مختاراً لما بين في كتاب الجنايات . وإن بدليل ما قال
He (the author) said: '(And if his master sold him while informing of the debt, then the creditors have the right to reject the sale)' because their right is attached to him, and it is the right of demanding earnings and satisfaction from his person, and in each there is benefit. The first is complete and deferred, and the second is incomplete and immediate. By the sale, this choice is lost, so they have the right to reject it. The benefit of informing of the debt is the dropping of the purchaser's option to return due to the defect of debt, so that the sale becomes binding between the two contracting parties, even though it is not binding with respect to the creditors. This is when the debt is due and the sale is without the creditors' request and the price does not satisfy their debts. If their debt is deferred, the sale is permissible because he sold his own property and he is able to deliver it, and no right of another is attached to it because the creditors' right is delayed, contrary to the due case. In al-Nihayah: He added: 'or if the creditors consent to the sale, then they have no right to reject.' This is unlike a pledge with deferred debt, where it is not permissible for him to sell it because the pledgee owns the corpus and cannot deliver it. The creditors have no hand in the ma'dhun slave nor in his earnings. And if nothing of what we mentioned exists, such as deferment of the price and the creditors' request for fulfillment of the price with the debt, then the sale is suspended until it becomes permissible by ratification, as per the issue of the book as we explained. No. And Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl that it is void (batil), and they differed regarding the creditors and its meaning: some said its meaning is that it will be void because a third party has the right to invalidate it; some said its meaning is that it is corrupted (fasid): if the purchaser after taking possession frees him or makes him tadbir, that is valid and he is liable for his value. Al-Asl: And in al-Inayah: If it is said: When the master sells his slave who committed a crime after knowing of the crime, he becomes bound for the ransom, so why is he not here bound to pay the debt from his wealth? The answer: Because the consequence of the crime is payment upon the master, and if it becomes impossible for him to sell, he is demanded for it due to the obligation remaining. As for the debt, it is an obligation upon the slave's dhimmah such that it does not drop by sale or emancipation, so it is exacted after freedom. Since that is so, the master's sale is akin to saying 'I will pay his debt', and that is a promise of charity, so it is not binding upon him. There is consideration in this because his saying 'I will pay his debt' could be suretyship, so it is not necessarily a promise. The answer is that a promise is the lesser of the two probabilities, so it is established by it since there is no evidence for the other. And if the ma'dhun slave commits a crime, the master is demanded for payment or ransom because the defendant regarding the person of the ma'dhun is the master, because it is the master's earning, not the ma'dhun's. For this reason, if someone claims a right in his person, the master is the defendant, not the ma'dhun. Similarly, the person of the indebted ma'dhun is not pursued except in the presence of the master, hoping he chooses ransom, because permission to the slave does not disable the owner from paying, since he remains under his ownership. And any disposition by the owner in the slave that does not disable him from payment does not make him choose [ransom], as explained in the book of jinayat. And by what is said as proof...
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling when a master sells his ma'dhun slave while informing the purchaser of the slave's debts. The key point is that the creditors can reject the sale because their right is attached to the slave's ability to earn and their right to satisfaction from his person. Informing the purchaser of the debt eliminates the purchaser's option to return the slave for the defect of debt, making the sale binding between master and buyer, but still subject to creditors' rejection. This ruling only applies if the debt is due and the sale price does not cover the debts. If the debt is deferred, the sale is valid because the creditors' right is delayed. The commentary from al-Nihayah adds that if the creditors consent to the sale, they cannot later reject it. A distinction is made with a pledged item: a pledgor cannot sell a pledged item with deferred debt because the pledgee's right prevents delivery. The text then notes that Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Asl considers this sale void (batil), but some interpret it as corrupted (fasid); if the purchaser frees or makes tadbir, it is valid but he must pay the value. An objection from al-Inayah is addressed: why isn't the master bound to pay the debt like he is for a jinyah? The answer explains that jinyah creates an immediate obligation on the master, while debt is on the slave's dhimmah; the master's sale is only a promise, not binding. The passage ends with a note on jinyah: if the ma'dhun commits a crime, the master is the defendant, and the slave's person is not pursued without the master present.

Key Terms
استسعاء (istis'a'): The right to make the slave earn money to pay off his debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can creditors reject the sale of a ma'dhun slave?
A) Whenever the master sells without informing
B) Only if the debt is due, sale not at creditors' request, and price insufficient
C) Only if the debt is deferred
D) Only if the master refuses to pay the debts

Key Takeaway
Creditors can reject a sale of a ma'dhun slave only when the debt is due, the sale is without their request, and the price does not cover the debt.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Validity of the Sale and Qiyas with Jinyah
Intra-madhhab dispute and analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of the sale (batil vs. fasid) and explain the qiyas that distinguishes jinyah from debt.

Core Idea
Muhammad al-Shaybani considers the sale void (batil); others consider it fasid; the master's liability for jinyah differs from debt.

This lesson delves into the disagreement among Hanafi scholars about the legal categorization of this sale. Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Asl calls it void (batil), while others interpret it as corrupt (fasid). We also examine the qiyas argument from al-Inayah comparing it to jinyah, and why the master is not obligated to pay the slave's debts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Muhammad al-Shaybani states the sale is batil. Others interpret it as fasid, meaning if the purchaser later frees the slave, it is valid but he must pay the value.
The difference affects the consequences: batil has no effect, fasid may be validated by subsequent acts.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
Al-Inayah raises: if the master sells a slave who committed jinyah, he is bound to pay the ransom. Why is he not also bound to pay the debts here?
3
Analyze the response
The response: jinyah obligates the master directly (the obligation is on him). Debt is an obligation on the slave's dhimmah; the master's sale is like a promise to pay, not binding.
The master's act is akin to a promise of charity (wa'd al-tabarru'), not legally binding.

Source Text

Key Terms
دين مؤجل (dayn mu'ajjal): A debt with a deferred term; not yet due.
باطل (batil): Void; legally null with no effect.
فاسد (fasid): Corrupt; a contract that can be validated by subsequent acts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the master's liability for jinyah from his liability for the slave's debts?
A) Jinyah is intentional; debt is unintentional
B) Jinyah creates an obligation on the master; debt is on the slave's dhimmah
C) Jinyah is criminal; debt is civil
D) Jinyah is forgiven by the buyer; debt is not

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on the mawquf view: creditors can reject the sale, but their consent validates it, unlike jinyah where the master is directly liable.

Jinyah and Debt: Master's Liability Distinguished
Distinction in liability between jinyah and debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the master is not liable for the slave's debts but is liable for jinyah, and apply the rule that the master's sale is a non-binding promise.

Core Idea
The master's liability for jinyah is direct; for debt, only the slave's dhimmah is burdened.

This lesson focuses on the comparison between jinyah (criminal act) and debt in the context of a ma'dhun slave. The master is directly liable for the slave's crimes but not for his debts. We explore the reasoning behind this distinction and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Compare the nature of jinyah and debt
Jinyah (criminal act) creates an immediate obligation on the master (wajib 'ala al-mawla). Debt (dayn) is an obligation on the slave's dhimmah, which is not transferred to the master.
2
Analyze the consequence of sale
When the master sells a slave who committed jinyah, the master remains liable and must pay ransom. When he sells a ma'dhun with debt, he is not liable; the debt follows the slave.
3
Understand the promise argument
The master's sale is analogous to a promise to pay the debt. A promise (wa'd) is not legally binding unless it is a surety (kafala). Since there is no evidence for surety, it is considered a mere promise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن باعه سيده وأعلم بالدين فللغرماء رد البيع) لأن حقهم تعلق به وهو حق الاستسعاء والاستيفاء من رقبته وفي كل منهما فائدة فالأول تام مؤخر والثاني ناقص معجل وبالبيع تفوت هذه الخيرة وكان فلما كان كذلك كان البيع من المولى بمنزلة أن يقال أنا أقضي دينه وذلك عدة بالتبرع فلا يلزمه وفيه نظر لأن قوله أنا أقضى دينه ويحتمل الكفالة فلا يتعين عدة والجواب أن العدة أدنى الاحتمالين فيثبت به لأنه لا دليل على غيره، وإذا جنى العبد المأذون طولب المولى بالدفع أو الفداء لأن الخصم في رقبة العبد المأذون هو المولى لأنها كسب المولى لا كسب المأذون ، ولهذا لو ادعى إنسان في رقبته حقاً . المولى خصماً للمدعي لا للمأذون، وكذا لاتباع رقبة المأذون المديون إلا بحضرة المولى لعله يختار الفداء لأن الإذن للعبد لا يعجز الملك عن الدفع لأنه باقٍ على ملكه، وكل تصرف أصابه المالك في العبد فلا يعجزه عن الدفع لا يصير به مختاراً لما بين في كتاب الجنايات . وإن بدليل ما قال
He (the author) said: '(And if his master sold him while informing of the debt, then the creditors have the right to reject the sale)' because their right is attached to him, and it is the right of demanding earnings and satisfaction from his person, and in each there is benefit. The first is complete and deferred, and the second is incomplete and immediate. By the sale, this choice is lost, so they have the right to reject it. The benefit of informing of the debt is the dropping of the purchaser's option to return due to the defect of debt, so that the sale becomes binding between the two contracting parties, even though it is not binding with respect to the creditors. This is when the debt is due and the sale is without the creditors' request and the price does not satisfy their debts. If their debt is deferred, the sale is permissible because he sold his own property and he is able to deliver it, and no right of another is attached to it because the creditors' right is delayed, contrary to the due case. In al-Nihayah: He added: 'or if the creditors consent to the sale, then they have no right to reject.' This is unlike a pledge with deferred debt, where it is not permissible for him to sell it because the pledgee owns the corpus and cannot deliver it. The creditors have no hand in the ma'dhun slave nor in his earnings. And if nothing of what we mentioned exists, such as deferment of the price and the creditors' request for fulfillment of the price with the debt, then the sale is suspended until it becomes permissible by ratification, as per the issue of the book as we explained. No. And Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl that it is void (batil), and they differed regarding the creditors and its meaning: some said its meaning is that it will be void because a third party has the right to invalidate it; some said its meaning is that it is corrupted (fasid): if the purchaser after taking possession frees him or makes him tadbir, that is valid and he is liable for his value. Al-Asl: And in al-Inayah: If it is said: When the master sells his slave who committed a crime after knowing of the crime, he becomes bound for the ransom, so why is he not here bound to pay the debt from his wealth? The answer: Because the consequence of the crime is payment upon the master, and if it becomes impossible for him to sell, he is demanded for it due to the obligation remaining. As for the debt, it is an obligation upon the slave's dhimmah such that it does not drop by sale or emancipation, so it is exacted after freedom. Since that is so, the master's sale is akin to saying 'I will pay his debt', and that is a promise of charity, so it is not binding upon him. There is consideration in this because his saying 'I will pay his debt' could be suretyship, so it is not necessarily a promise. The answer is that a promise is the lesser of the two probabilities, so it is established by it since there is no evidence for the other. And if the ma'dhun slave commits a crime, the master is demanded for payment or ransom because the defendant regarding the person of the ma'dhun is the master, because it is the master's earning, not the ma'dhun's. For this reason, if someone claims a right in his person, the master is the defendant, not the ma'dhun. Similarly, the person of the indebted ma'dhun is not pursued except in the presence of the master, hoping he chooses ransom, because permission to the slave does not disable the owner from paying, since he remains under his ownership. And any disposition by the owner in the slave that does not disable him from payment does not make him choose [ransom], as explained in the book of jinayat. And by what is said as proof...
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling when a master sells his ma'dhun slave while informing the purchaser of the slave's debts. The key point is that the creditors can reject the sale because their right is attached to the slave's ability to earn and their right to satisfaction from his person. Informing the purchaser of the debt eliminates the purchaser's option to return the slave for the defect of debt, making the sale binding between master and buyer, but still subject to creditors' rejection. This ruling only applies if the debt is due and the sale price does not cover the debts. If the debt is deferred, the sale is valid because the creditors' right is delayed. The commentary from al-Nihayah adds that if the creditors consent to the sale, they cannot later reject it. A distinction is made with a pledged item: a pledgor cannot sell a pledged item with deferred debt because the pledgee's right prevents delivery. The text then notes that Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Asl considers this sale void (batil), but some interpret it as corrupted (fasid); if the purchaser frees or makes tadbir, it is valid but he must pay the value. An objection from al-Inayah is addressed: why isn't the master bound to pay the debt like he is for a jinyah? The answer explains that jinyah creates an immediate obligation on the master, while debt is on the slave's dhimmah; the master's sale is only a promise, not binding. The passage ends with a note on jinyah: if the ma'dhun commits a crime, the master is the defendant, and the slave's person is not pursued without the master present.

Key Terms
جناية (jinyah): A criminal act causing harm; in this context, an act that gives the victim a right against the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the master the defendant in litigation involving a ma'dhun slave's jinyah?
A) Because the slave is not a legal person
B) Because the slave's person is the master's earning
C) Because the master must protect the slave
D) Because the slave cannot speak in court

Key Takeaway
The master is directly liable for the ma'dhun slave's jinyah but not for his debts; the slave's person is the master's earning, but debts remain on the slave's dhimmah.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Sale of a Ma'dhun Slave: Creditors' Right to Rescind
Ruling with proof on master's sale of ma'dhun slave and creditors' rescission right.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the master's sale of a ma'dhun slave with due debt, identify the condition that triggers creditors' rescission, and distinguish between the majority view and Imam Muhammad's position.

Core Idea
The master's sale of a ma'dhun slave is valid but creditors may rescind if the debt is due and unpaid.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator explains the ruling when a master sells his ma'dhun slave (a slave permitted to trade) while the slave owes a debt. The key issue is the creditors' right to rescind the sale. We will unpack the conditions, the underlying reasoning, and an intra-Hanafi difference with Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
The master sells his ma'dhun slave, informing the buyer of the debt. The creditors are third parties with a right to the slave's earnings (istis'a').
2
Determine the default ruling
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the sale is valid between the master and buyer, but the creditors have the right to rescind. It is a suspended sale (mawquf).
Intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad says void.
3
Identify the condition for rescission
The debt must be due (hal), the sale not at the creditors' request, and the price insufficient to cover the debt. If the debt is deferred or creditors consent, the sale becomes fully binding.
4
Trace the proof: creditors' right
The creditors' right is attached to the slave's earning capacity and collection from his person. By selling, the master destroys the creditors' option to choose between complete but deferred (istis'a') and incomplete but immediate (collection), so they may rescind.
The proof is rational (talil), not a direct text.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad holds the sale is void (batil) or corrupt (fasid). Under his view, if the buyer emancipates after possession, it is valid but the buyer must pay the slave's value.
The thamarah al-khilaf: the effect of subsequent emancipation.
6
Distinguish from a slave who committed a crime
In a crime, liability is on the master (surrender or ransom); for debt, liability stays on the slave. Thus the master's promise to pay is a gratuitous promise (wa'd) not binding.
Shows depth of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن باعه سيده وأعلم بالدين فللغرماء رد البيع) لأن حقهم تعلق به وهو حق الاستسعاء والاستيفاء من رقبته وفي كل منهما فائدة فالأول تام مؤخر والثاني ناقص معجل وبالبيع تفوت هذه الخيرة وكان لهم رده وفائدة الإعلام بالدين سقوط خيار المشتري في الرد بعيب الدين حتى يلزم البيع في حق المتعاقدين وإن لم يكن لازماً في حق الغرماء. هذا إذا كان الدين حالاً وكان البيع من غير طلب الغرماء والثمن لا يوفي بديونهم، وإن كان دينهم مؤجلاً فالبيع جائز لأنه باع ملكه وهو قادر على تسليمه ولم يتعلق به حق لغيره لأن حق الغرماء يتأخر بخلاف الحال. وفي النهاية : زاد أو رضي الغرماء بالبيع فلا يكون لهم الرد، وهذا بخلاف الرهن بالدين المؤجل حيث لا يجوز له أن يبيعه لأن المرتهن ملك الرقبة فلا يقدر على تسليمه ولا يد للغرماء في العبد المأذون ولا في كسبه، وإذا لم يوجد شيء مما ذكرنا من تأجيل الثمن وطلب الغرماء وفاء الثمن بالدين فالبيع موقوف حتى يجوز بإجازة مسألة الكتاب على ما بينا . لا وذكر محمد في الأصل أنه باطل واختلفوا في الغرماء وهي معناه ؛ فقال بعضهم معناء إنه سيبطل لأن للغير حق ابطاله وقال بعضهم معناه إنه فاسد : إنه إذا أعتقه المشتري بعد القبض أو دبره صح ذلك ويلزمه قيمته . الأصل : في وفي العناية : فإن قيل : إذا باع المولى عبده الجاني بعد العلم بالجناية كان مختاراً الفداء فما باله ههنا لا يكون مختار القضاء الدين من ماله؟ الجواب بأن موجب الجناية الدفع على المولى فإذا تعذر عليه بالبيع طولب به لبقاء الواجب عليه، وأما الدين فهو واجب في ذمة العبد بحيث لا يسقط عنه بالبيع والاعتاق حتى يؤخذ به بعد العتق فلما كان كذلك كان البيع من المولى بمنزلة أن يقال أنا أقضي دينه وذلك عدة بالتبرع فلا يلزمه وفيه نظر لأن قوله أنا أقضى دينه ويحتمل الكفالة فلا يتعين عدة والجواب أن العدة أدنى الاحتمالين فيثبت به لأنه لا دليل على غيره.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if his master sold him and informed of the debt, then the creditors have the right to rescind the sale) because their right is attached to him, which is the right of seeking earnings and collection from his person, and in each there is benefit: the first is complete but deferred, the second is incomplete and immediate. By the sale, this choice is lost, so they have the right to rescind. The benefit of informing of the debt is the dropping of the buyer's option to return due to the defect of debt, so the sale is binding for the contractors although not binding for the creditors. This is when the debt is due and the sale is without the request of the creditors and the price does not cover their debts. If their debt is deferred, the sale is valid because he sold his property and he is able to deliver it, and no right of others is attached to it because the right of the creditors is deferred, unlike the case of a due debt. In al-Nihaya: If the creditors increased or agreed to the sale, they have no right to rescind. This is contrary to the case of a pledge for a deferred debt, where he is not permitted to sell it because the pledgee owns the corpus, so he cannot deliver, and the creditors have no hand in the ma'dhun slave nor in his earnings. If none of what we mentioned of delay of price and request of creditors for payment of debt exists, the sale is suspended until it is permitted by authorization of the subject of the book as we explained. No, and Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl that it is void, and they differed regarding the creditors: some said it means it will be void because another has the right to invalidate it; some said it means it is corrupt: that if the buyer emancipates him after possession or makes him mudabbar, that is valid and he must pay his value. Al-Asl: and in al-Inaya: If it is said: When the master sells his slave who has committed a crime after knowing the crime, he becomes liable for ransom; why here is he not liable for payment of debt from his own wealth? The answer is that the liability of crime is surrender on the master, and if it becomes impossible by sale, he is demanded due to the remaining obligation. As for debt, it is obligatory on the slave's liability such that it does not drop by sale or emancipation until he is taken after emancipation. Therefore, the sale by the master is equivalent to saying 'I will pay his debt', and that is a gratuitous promise, so it is not binding. There is consideration because his saying 'I will pay his debt' may imply suretyship, so it is not definitely a promise; the answer is that a promise is the lesser of the two probabilities, so it is established because there is no evidence for the other.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The matn states: if the master sells the ma'dhun slave after informing the buyer of the debt, the creditors have the right to rescind the sale. The commentator explains that the creditors' right is attached to the slave's earning capacity (istis'a') and the ability to collect from his person. By selling, the master destroys that right, so the creditors can rescind. Informing the buyer drops the buyer's option to return due to the defect of debt, making the sale binding between master and buyer but not against creditors. The ruling applies when the debt is due, the sale is not at the creditors' request, and the price does not cover the debts. If the debt is deferred, the sale is valid because the master can deliver and no immediate right is attached. If creditors consent, they lose the right to rescind. A distinction is made with a pledge (rahn) where the pledgee cannot sell a pledged item. The sale is suspended if it doesn't meet the stated conditions. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani is reported to say the sale is void, not merely suspended, leading to an intra-Hanafi dispute. A question is then posed: why is the master not liable for the debt as he is for a crime? The answer is that the master's liability for a crime is direct, whereas debt is on the slave's liability; the master's promise to pay is a gratuitous promise not binding. The discussion of probabilities (ihtimalat) shows the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Key Terms
الغرماء (al-ghuramā'): The creditors who have a claim against the ma'dhun slave's earnings.
الاستسعاء (al-istis‘ā’): The right to make the slave work to earn money to pay off debts.
البيع الموقوف (al-bay‘ al-mawqūf): A sale that is valid but incomplete, pending approval of a third party (here the creditors).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The debt is deferred.
B) The price covers the debt.
C) The sale is at creditors' request.
D) The debt is due and the price does not cover it.

Key Takeaway
The master's sale of a ma'dhun slave with due debt is valid but subject to creditors' rescission to protect their right to the slave's earnings.

Effect of Master's Confession of Debt vs. Jinaaya on Ma'dhun Slave
Comparison of master's confession on debt vs. crime

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi rulings on a master's confession of debt and confession of a crime (jinaaya) regarding a ma'dhun slave, and apply the underlying principle that ties the validity of confession to the effect on creditors' rights.

Core Idea
The master's confession of debt is not accepted if it harms creditors, but confession of crime is accepted because crime liability does not block ownership transfer.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author explains a critical distinction in Hanafi fiqh: the validity of a master's confession regarding his ma'dhun slave depends on whether the confessed claim hinders the master's ability to transfer ownership. If the slave has debt, the master's confession of a debt is not accepted—because creditors have a prior claim on the slave's value, and the master cannot jeopardize that. In contrast, a confession of a crime (jinaaya) is accepted, because the crime does not prevent sale or gift. The reasoning (talil) hinges on the nature of the right—debt attaches to wealth (maliyyah), while crime attaches to liability (dhimma). We will unpack this and its implications for mu'amalat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) master confesses debt then crime; (2) master confesses second crime when first crime known. Note the different outcomes.
Focus on the legal status of the confession in each case.
2
Find the ruling for each
In debt case: master not believed unless he pays debt. In crime case: confession is valid.
Memorize the specific phrasing: 'لم يصدق إلا أن يقضي دينه' vs 'صح إقراره'.
3
Locate the legal reasoning (talil)
The author gives a 'farq' (difference): debt prevents transfer of ownership because creditors can rescind; crime does not prevent transfer.
The key phrase: 'والفرق أن دين العبد يمنع المولى من تمليك العبد من غيره إلا برضا الغرماء'.
4
Trace the analogies used
The author analogizes to sale/gift (rescindable for debt, not for crime) and to nisab of zakat (owner can still sell but liable if wasted).
Analyze why these analogies support the distinction.
5
Apply to the sulh (compromise) scenario
The master attempts a compromise by giving the slave to crime victims in lieu of qisas. The Hanafi ruling: invalid because it harms creditors. The slave is sold for debt first.
Understand the consequence: qisas drops but slave is sold for creditors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على عبده المأذون دين معروف أو أقر به المولى ثم أقر عليه بجناية لم يصدق إلا أن يقضي دينه، ولو كان عليه جناية معروفة وأقر المولى على عبده بالجناية الثانية صح إقراره . والفرق أن دين العبد يمنع المولى من تمليك العبد من غيره إلا برضا الغرماء ألا ترى لو باعه أو وهبه كان لهم أن ينقضوا فكذا يمنع صحة الإقرار بما يوجب تمليكه من غيره، فأما جناية العبد لا تمنع المولى من تمليك العبد من غيره ألا ترى لو باعه أو وهبه من غيره صح ولم يكن لولي الجناية نقضه وفقهه أن دين العبد واجب في ذمة العبد لا في ذمة المولى وتعلق بماليته والمالية في العبد والحق الثابت في العين عجز المالك من تمليكه من غيره لا يتضمن إبطال حق الغير كحق المرتهن في الرهن فأما موجب جناية العبد تجب في ذمة المولى وهو الدفع أو الفداء إلا أنه يتعلق بالعبد وهو دفعه ولا يتعلق بماليته، وإذا كان موجب الجناية يتعلق بذمة المولى فلا يحجزه عن التصرف فيه لأنه تصرف في محل خالص له لا حق للغير فيه إلا أنه إذا استهلكه ضمن قيمته لأن العبد محل إقامة حقهم وهو الدفع فصار كنصاب الزكاة وجبت فيه الزكاة ولا يحجر المالك عن التصرف فيه وإذا استهلكه ضمن فكذا هذا. ولو قتل رجلاً عمداً وعليه دين فصالح المولى على أن جعل العبد لأصحاب الجناية بحقهم لم يجز وليس لهم أن يقتلوه وقد سقط القصاص ويباع في الدين، فإن فضل شيء كان لصاحب الجناية وإلا فلا شيء له على أحد أبداً لأن تملك المولى العبد من ولي القصاص بالصلح لو صح يؤدي إلى إبطال حق الغرماء وفي المحيط : محجور اشترى ثوباً ولم يعلم مولاه بذلك حتى باع العبد
On his ma'dhun slave there is a known debt or the master confessed to it, then he confessed to a crime on him, he is not believed unless he pays his debt. And if there was a known crime on him and the master confessed to a second crime on his slave, his confession is valid. The difference is that the slave's debt prevents the master from transferring ownership of the slave to others except with the creditors' consent. Do you not see that if he sold him or gifted him, they could rescind? Likewise, it prevents the validity of a confession that necessitates transferring him to others. As for the slave's crime, it does not prevent the master from transferring ownership of the slave to others. Do you not see that if he sold him or gifted him to another, it is valid and the guardian of the crime cannot rescind it? The juristic reasoning is that the slave's debt is obligatory on the slave's liability, not on the master's liability, and it attaches to his wealth, and the wealth is in the slave. The right established in a physical object disables the owner from transferring it to others without implying abrogation of the other's right, like the right of the pledgee in a pledge. As for the liability of the slave's crime, it is obligatory on the master's liability, which is surrender or ransom, but it attaches to the slave through surrender, and it does not attach to his wealth. Since the liability of the crime attaches to the master's liability, it does not prevent him from disposing of it because it is a disposal in a place purely his own with no right of others in it. However, if he destroys it, he becomes liable for its value because the slave is the locus of their right, namely surrender, so it becomes like the nisab of zakat: zakat is due on it but does not prevent the owner from disposing of it; if he destroys it, he is liable. So likewise this. And if he killed a man intentionally and he had debt, then the master made a compromise by making the slave belong to the crime victims for their right, it is not permitted, and they may not kill him, and qisas is dropped, and the slave is sold for the debt. If anything remains, it is for the crime victims; otherwise, they have nothing against anyone ever because if the master's ownership of the slave from the qisas guardian by compromise were valid, it would lead to abrogation of the creditors' right. And in al-Muhit: A restricted slave bought a garment without his master's knowledge until the master sold the slave...
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between the master's confession of debt and confession of crime (jinaaya) regarding a ma'dhun slave. The master's confession of debt is not accepted if it leads to transferring the slave to others without creditors' consent, because debt attaches to the slave's value. In contrast, confession of crime is accepted because the master's liability for crime (surrender or ransom) does not prevent sale or gift; the master can still transfer ownership, but if he destroys the slave, he must pay its value. The analogy with zakat nisab is used: a person can sell the nisab even though zakat is due; if he wastes it, he guarantees the zakat. Similarly, the master can sell the slave despite the crime liability. A further ruling: if a slave commits murder (intentional) and has debt, a compromise by the master giving the slave to the victim's family for the right of qisas is invalid because it would harm creditors; instead, qisas drops (since the slave is not killed due to creditors' right?) and the slave is sold for debt, with any surplus to the crime victims. The final sentence from al-Muhit appears incomplete but introduces the case of a restricted slave (mahjoor) buying a garment without the master's knowledge until the master sells the slave.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrār): The master's admission of a claim (debt or crime) against his ma'dhun slave.
الصلح (al-ṣulḥ): A compromise between the master and crime victims to transfer the slave in lieu of qisas.
المحجور (al-maḥjūr): A slave restricted from trading (as opposed to ma'dhun), who may not buy without master's permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the master's confession of a second crime on the ma'dhun slave accepted despite the slave having a known crime?
A) Because the master owns the slave and can admit anything
B) Because crime liability does not prevent the master from transferring ownership
C) Because the creditors consent to it
D) Because the slave is not burdened by debt

Key Takeaway
The validity of a master's confession regarding a ma'dhun slave depends on whether the confessed claim attaches to the slave's wealth (debt) or the master's liability (crime); debt confession harms creditors and is invalid, while crime confession is valid.

Transactions of a Restricted Slave and Master's Authorization
Mixed rulings on mahjur slave transactions and authorization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of a restricted slave's transactions and explain how authorization or silence of the master affects their validity.

Core Idea
A restricted slave's transactions are suspended; silence of the master can make him authorized, but not ratification after the fact.

This lesson examines the legal capacity of a restricted slave (mahjur) to engage in buying, borrowing, and selling. We will see that such transactions are not automatically void but suspended (mawquf) pending the master's ratification. Silence in certain cases can imply authorization. Understanding these rulings is essential for appreciating the Hanafi principle of suspended contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction
Determine what the restricted slave did: bought, sold, borrowed, etc.
2
Check the slave's status
Is the slave mahjur (restricted) or authorized (ma'dhun)? If authorized, transactions are valid; if restricted, they are suspended.
3
Analyze the master's involvement
Did the master authorize before, during, or after? Silence while observing can grant authorization. Explicit authorization after the act may not always validate a purchase.
Key: authorization of purchase after the act does not make the purchase valid.
4
Determine the ruling
State the final hukm: transaction valid, suspended, or invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن غاب البائع فالمشتري ليس بخصم لهم ومن قدم مصراً وقال عبد زيد فاشترى جاز لأن محجور رحمه . ثم أجاز شراءه لم يجز الشراء أبداً، ولو باع ثوباً من رجل ثم إن المولى باع العبد وأجاز البيع العبد لم يفسخ البيع الموقوف فالإجازة صادفت عبداً موقوفاً فصحت. عبد بيع أدان رجلاً فنهى مولاه من عليه الدين أن يدفع إلى العبد فقضى الغريم عين ما أخذه بريء عند الإمام في الوجهين لأن الإدانة من المحجور عليه موقوفة وحقوق العقد ترجع إلى العاقد في الثابت والموقوف جميعاً كما في الفضولي إذا أدان ماله غيره فقضاه المديون برئ فكذا هذا. وفي المحيط : عبد محجور عليه دين اكتسب دراهم بغير إذن السيد واشترى بها ثوباً والسيد ينظر إليه فسكت صار العبد مأذوناً في التجارة وللمولى أن يرجع بالدراهم على البائع ويرجع البائع بالدارهم ديناً على العبد محجور اشترى داراً وباعها ثم بلغ المولى فأجاز البيع والشراء قال يجوز الشراء ولا يجوز البيع لأنه لما أجاز الشراء فقد ظهر ملك المولى باتاً على موقوف فأبطله وفي المحيط : أسروا العبد المأذون وأحرزوه ثم ظهر المسلمون عليهم أخذه مولاه بغير شيء قبل القسمة وبعدها بقيمته فإن كان جنى جناية وكان عليه دين لزماه. قال الله : ( وإن غاب البائع فالمشتري ليس بخصم لهم يعني لو باع المولى عبده المأذون المديون وقبض الثمن وتسلمه المشتري ثم غاب البائع لا يكون المشتري خصماً للغرماء إذا أنكر المشتري الدين وهذا عند الإمام ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف: يكون المشتري خصماً ويقضي لهم بدينهم، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا اشترى داراً وهبها وسلمها إليه ثم غاب المشتري والواهب ثم حضر الشفيع فالموهوب له لا يكون خصماً عندهما خلافاً له هو يقول: إن ذا اليد يدعي الملك لنفسه في العين فيكون خصماً فيها كما لو ادعى ملك العبد. ولهما أن الدعوى تقتضي فسخ العقد وهو قائم بالبائع والمشتري فيكون الفسخ قضاء على الغائب والحاضر ليس بخصم عنه بخلاف ما إذا ادعى الملك لأن صاحب اليد يظهر في الانتهاء أنه كان غاصباً منهم والغاصب يكون خصماً، وبخلاف دعوى الرهن لأن فيه فائدة لأن الرهن لا يباع ولو صدق المشتري في الدين كان للغرماء أن يردوا المبيع بالإجماع لأن إقراره حجة عليه فيفسخ بيعه إذا لم يوف الثمن بديونهم، ولو كان البائع حاضراً والمشتري غائباً فلا خصومة بينهم وبين البائع بالإجماع حتى يحضر المشتري لأن الملك واليد للمشتري ولا يمكن وهو غائب إبطالهما لكن لهم أن يضمنوا البائع قيمته لأنه صار ملزماً بحقهم بالبيع والتسليم، فإذا ضمنوه القيمة جاز البيع وكان الثمن للبائع وإن اختاروا إجازة البيع أخذوا الثمن، ولو قال إذا غاب أحدهما فالحاضر ليس بخصم إذا أنكر لكان أولى اهـ .
If the seller is absent, the buyer is not an opponent for them. And whoever comes to Egypt and says 'I am the slave of Zaid' and buys and sells, it is valid because [he is] a restricted slave. Then he authorized his purchase: the purchase is never valid. If he sold a garment to a man, then the master sold the slave and the slave authorized the sale: the suspended sale is not rescinded, because the authorization struck a suspended slave, so it is valid. A slave who was sold borrowed from a man; his master forbade the debtor from paying the slave; but the debtor paid exactly what he had taken: he is discharged according to Imam in both cases, because the loan from a restricted slave is suspended, and the rights of the contract go back to the contractor in both the established and suspended, like the case of a fuduli: if someone with no authority lends his property to another and the debtor pays, he is discharged. So also this. And in al-Muhit: A restricted slave owed a debt; he earned dirhams without the master's permission and bought a garment with them while the master looked on and remained silent: the slave becomes authorized in trade. The master may take back the dirhams from the seller, and the seller takes back the dirhams as a debt from the restricted slave. A restricted slave bought a house and sold it; then the master became of age and authorized the sale and purchase: he said the purchase is valid but the sale is not, because when he authorized the purchase, it appeared that the master's ownership was established definitively over the suspended, so he invalidated it. And in al-Muhit: The authorized slave was captured and taken away; then the Muslims overcame them: the master takes him without anything before division, and after division with its value. If he committed a crime and had a debt, both are binding on him. He said: If the seller is absent, the buyer is not an opponent for them. That is, if the master sold his authorized slave who is in debt and took the price and the buyer took possession, then the seller became absent: the buyer is not an opponent for the creditors if the buyer denies the debt. This is according to Imam and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: the buyer is an opponent and judgment is given against him for their debt. And this dispute applies if he bought a house, gave it as a gift, and handed it over, then the buyer and the donor became absent, then the preemptor came: the donee is not an opponent according to them, contrary to him. He says: the possessor claims ownership for himself in the property, so he is an opponent in it, just as if he claimed ownership of the slave. For them, the claim requires annulment of the contract, which is pending on the seller and buyer, so the annulment would be a judgment against the absent person, and the present is not an opponent for him. Unlike if he claimed ownership, because the possessor appears eventually to have been a usurper from them, and the usurper is an opponent. And unlike a claim of mortgage because there is benefit: the mortgage is not sold. If the buyer confirms the debt, the creditors may return the sold item by consensus, because his admission is proof against him, so his sale is annulled if the price does not cover their debts. If the seller is present and the buyer is absent, there is no litigation between them and the seller by consensus until the buyer is present, because ownership and possession are with the buyer, and it is impossible to annul them in his absence. But they may claim the value from the seller because he became liable to them by the sale and delivery. If they claim the value, the sale becomes valid and the price goes to the seller. If they choose to ratify the sale, they take the price. It was said: if one of them is absent, the present is not an opponent if he denies, which is more appropriate. End.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage from page 191 covers several rulings. First, it addresses a restricted slave's transactions: a loan from a restricted slave is suspended; if the master authorizes after the fact, it may not make the purchase valid. A silent observation by the master can turn a restricted slave into an authorized one. Then it moves to a major intra-Hanafi dispute: when a slave is sold to pay debts and the seller (master) disappears, can the creditors sue the buyer? According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, no, because the buyer is not an opponent for the absent master's contract; the creditors can only go after the master. Abu Yusuf holds that the buyer is an opponent because he is in possession. The commentary also extends this to a gift and pre-emption case, and discusses when the seller is present but the buyer absent. The relied-upon position is that the present party is not an opponent if he denies the debt.

Key Terms
محجور (mahjur): A slave restricted from trading by his master; not authorized.
مأذون (ma'dhun): An authorized slave who can trade freely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a restricted slave (mahjur) buys a garment and later the master authorizes the purchase, what is the ruling?
A) The purchase becomes valid.
B) The purchase is never valid.
C) The purchase is valid only if the price is paid.
D) The purchase is valid only if the slave is also authorized to sell.

Key Takeaway
A restricted slave's purchase authorized after the fact is invalid, but silent observation by the master grants trading authority.

intermediate
8 min
The Buyer as Opponent (Khasm) When the Seller is Absent: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on buyer as opponent for creditors when seller absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position (Imam Muhammad and Abu Hanifa) on whether the buyer is a valid opponent for creditors when the seller is absent, and evaluate Abu Yusuf's counter-argument.

Core Idea
The buyer who denies the debt is not an opponent because annulment would affect the absent seller.

This lesson delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about civil litigation involving a sold slave with debts. When the master-seller disappears, can the creditors sue the buyer? Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad say no, while Imam Abu Yusuf argues yes. This debate also extends to pre-emption cases. Understanding the reasoning behind each position sharpens your grasp of Hanafi principles of contract and claim.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
A master sells his authorized but debt-ridden slave, takes the price, and disappears. The buyer denies the debts. Creditors want to sue the buyer.
2
Identify the two Hanafi positions
Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the buyer is not a valid opponent (khasm). Abu Yusuf says he is.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's proof
He argues that the buyer is in possession and claims ownership, so he is like a usurper who can be sued.
4
Analyze the response of Imam and Muhammad
They argue that the creditors' claim is to rescind the sale, which would affect the absent seller. The buyer is not an opponent for the seller.
Also, if the buyer admits the debt, creditors can return the sale by consensus.
5
Apply to pre-emption case
The same dispute applies: a donee of a house is not an opponent for the pre-emptor according to Imam and Muhammad, because the gift is a contract involving the absent donor.

This passage from page 191 covers several rulings. محجور (mahjur): A slave restricted from trading by his master; not authorized.
شفيع (shafi'): A pre-emptor entitled to claim a sold property by right of proximity.

Slave's Claim of Authorization Accepted by Istihsan
Ruling that a slave's statement of being authorized is accepted for his trade debts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts a slave's claim of authorization by istihsan and distinguish it from the qiyas ruling.

Core Idea
Istihsan overrides qiyas to facilitate commerce: a slave's word is accepted without proof.

In Hanafi fiqh, a slave who engages in trade often needs permission from his master. But what if the slave simply claims he has permission? This lesson explores a specific ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq where the Hanafi school, by way of istihsan, accepts the slave's word to ensure smooth transactions. We will see how this practical need overrides the stricter qiyas reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the passage and identify the ruling: a slave who comes to a city and claims to be authorized to trade is believed; his trade is binding and debts are paid from his earnings.
The ruling applies whether the slave is just or not.
2
Contrast qiyas and istihsan
Note that qiyas would not accept the slave's claim because it is a self-serving statement that could harm the master. But istihsan accepts it. Understand that istihsan is a source of law in the Hanafi school used to avoid hardship.
Istihsan literally means 'to deem something better'.
3
Understand the rationale for istihsan
The rationale is to facilitate commerce. Without accepting the slave's word, trade would be difficult because verifying permission from a distant master is impractical.
This is a classic application of istihsan overriding qiyas.
4
Apply to a similar scenario
Consider another scenario: a hired worker claims his employer allowed him to purchase supplies. By analogy with this ruling, his claim might be accepted by istihsan to avoid disruption of business.
The principle is that necessity and custom (urf) can override strict analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ومن قدم مصراً وقال عبد زيد فاشترى وباع لزمه كل شيء من التجارة) يعني يقبل قوله في الإذن في حق كسبه حتى تقضى بها ديونه والمسألة على وجهين: أحدهما أن يخبر أن المولى أذن له فيصدق استحساناً عدلاً كان أو غير عدل، والقياس أن لا يصدق.
He (the author) said: Whoever comes to Egypt and says 'I am the slave of Zaid' and buys and sells, everything from trade is binding on him. That is, his word regarding the permission for his earnings is accepted, so that his debts are paid from it. The issue has two aspects: one is that he informs that the master authorized him, and he is believed by istihsan, whether he is just or not. By qiyas, he is not believed.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: if a slave comes to a city and declares himself to be the slave of Zaid, and engages in trade, all his trade transactions are binding on him. The commentary explains that his claim of being authorized is accepted by istihsan (juristic preference), even if he is not a just witness. The qiyas (analogy) would require that his word not be accepted since it is a claim that could harm the master. But istihsan prevails because of the need for smooth commerce and the difficulty of verifying permission. This is a clear example of istihsan overriding qiyas in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from strict qiyas for a more equitable outcome.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning based on an effective cause.
إذن (idhn): Permission from the master to the slave to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a slave's claim of being authorized to trade accepted by istihsan even if he is not a just person?
A) Because qiyas requires it
B) Because of the need for ease in commerce and difficulty in verifying permission
C) Because the master will later confirm
D) Because the slave is trustworthy by default

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a slave's claim of authorization for trade is accepted by istihsan to avoid hardship and facilitate commerce, overriding the stricter qiyas ruling.

Silent Permission and Istihsan for the Ma'dhun Slave
Commentary establishing silent permission by istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school establishes a slave's permission to trade through istihsan based on the master's silence and custom, and state the ruling on the validity of the slave's transactions.

Core Idea
Istihsan, based on consensus and necessity, validates a slave's trades even without explicit permission.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, the permission (idhn) for a slave to trade can be established not only by explicit statement but also by the master's silence and customary practice. This passage from al-Bahr al-Rai'q explains the legal basis for this ruling, relying on istihsan, which overrides strict qiyas due to necessity and widespread custom. Understanding this principle clarifies why the transactions of a seemingly permitted slave are considered valid and binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios for establishing permission
The passage first presents the case where the master is present and admits permission, and the second case where the slave trades without informing anyone; by istihsan, permission is presumed from the master's silence.
2
Recognize the role of istihsan
Istihsan is used to override the strict qiyas that would require explicit permission at every transaction. The basis is the consensus of Muslims (ijma) and the necessity of daily transactions.
Hanafis consider custom and necessity as grounds for istihsan.
3
Analyze the proof: ijma and necessity
The commentator cites ijma as a proof that custom can specify the athar and abandon qiyas. Also, the principle 'what has become narrow expands' supports the ruling.
This istihsan is based on a clear necessity (darura) and general need (balwa).
4
Understand the limitation on selling the slave's person
Even if the slave's earnings do not cover his debts, the master cannot be forced to sell the slave's person because it is his property, unlike earnings which the master does not own.
This protects the master's property rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وباع لزمه كل شيء من التجارة فإن حضر وأقر بالإذن بيع وإلا فلا وإن أذن للصبي أو المعتوه الذي يعقل البيع والشراء وليه فهو في الشراء والبيع كالعبد المأذون له. وجه الاستحسان أن الناس يعاملونه من غير اشتراط شيء من ذلك وإجماع المسلمين حجة يخص بها الأثر ويترك بها القياس ولأن في ذلك ضرورة وبلوى فإن الإذن لا بد منه لصحة تصرفه وإقامة الحجة عند كل عقد غير ممكن، وما ضاق على الناس أمره اتسع بحكمه، وما عمت بليته اتسعت قضيته والثاني أن يبيع ويشتري ولا يخبر بشيء ففي الاستحسان يثبت إذنه لأن الظاهر أنه مأذون لأن عقله ودينه يمنعه عن ارتكاب المحرم لأن الظاهر هو الأصل فيعمل به فصح تصرفاته، وإن لم يوف الكسب بالدين لاتباع رقبته لأنها ملك المولى فلا يصدق فيه ولا يلزم من وجوب الدين عليه أن تباع فيه كما لو كان المديون مدبراً أو أم ولد بخلاف الكسب. فإن المولى لا يملكه. وفي المحيط : لو جاء بأمة فقال هذه أمتي فبايعوها فولدت ثم استحقت ضمن لهم قيمتها وقيمة أولادها ولا يضمن ما وهب لها وما اكتسب ويضمن القيمة في كل يوم الاستحقاق لا يوم الغرور ولو قال لأهل السوق بايعوه ثم نهى واحداً أو اثنين عن مبايعته ثم استحق لم يضمن لمن نهاه لأن التخصيص في الحجر عن المبايعة صحيح، ولو دخل رجل بعبده من السوق وقال هذا عبدي وقد أذنت له في التجارة وقد لحقه دين ثم وجد حراً لم يكن غاراً، ولو قال بايعوه ضمن لهم الأقل من القيمة ومن الدين. والفرق أن الأمر بالمبايعة لا ينفك عن وجوب الدين والإذن ينفك . ولو قال هذا عبدي فبايعوه وقد أذنت له في التجار ولو لم يقل أذنت فهو غار ولو قال هذا مدبري قد أذنت له في التجارة فلحقه دين فاستحقه رجل لم يضمن الذي غرهم شيئاً . ولو قال بايعوه في البر ضمن إذا بايعوه في كل نوع ولو قال أذنت له في التجارة لأقوام بأعيانهم فبايعوه وغيرهم فوجد حراً أو مستحقاً للغير ضمن لمن أمره خاصة فإن قلت: قد تقدم أن العبد يباع في الدين إذا أذن له ولو لم يقل بايعوه وهنا لا يضمن إلا إذا قال أذنت وبايعوه. قلنا : هذا ضمان غرور فلهذا ضمن لم أمره خاصة قدر حصتهم لأن الناس يتفاوتون في المعاملات. ولو قال بايعوه ولم يقل إنه عبدي لم يكن غاراً ولم يضمن لأحد شيئاً، ولو كان الذي قال هذا عبدي صبياً أو مكاتباً أو مدبراً لا تجوز كفالته لم يضمن شيئاً ا وفيه: لو قال هذا ابني وقد أذنت له في التجارة فبايعوه وقد كان ابن غيره فهو غار لأنه أطمعهم أن ديونهم تجب في ذمة الصبي وتستوفى من ماله بسبب إذنه وقد ظهر الأمر بخلافه ا هـ
And he sold, everything from trade is binding. If the master is present and admits to the permission, [the slave] is sold [for debts]; otherwise not. And if the guardian gives permission to the minor or the insane person who understands buying and selling, he is in purchase and sale like the permitted slave. The basis of istihsan: people deal with him without stipulating any of that, and the consensus of Muslims is a proof by which the athar is specified and qiyas is abandoned, and because there is necessity and general need. Permission is indispensable for the validity of his transaction, and establishing proof at every contract is not possible. What has become narrow for people expands by its ruling, and whose calamity is widespread, its case expands. The second [case]: he sells and buys without informing anything. In istihsan his permission is established because the apparent state is that he is permitted, because his reason and religion prevent him from committing forbidden acts, and the apparent state is the basis, so it is acted upon; thus his transactions are valid. Even if the earnings do not cover the debt, his person is not sold because it is the master's property, so he is not believed in it, and the obligation of debt does not necessitate his sale, just as if the debtor was a mudabbar or umm walad, unlike earnings, for the master does not own them. In al-Muhit: If he brought a slave-girl and said: 'This is my slave-girl, so deal with her,' and she gave birth, then she was claimed, he guarantees for them her value and the value of her children, and he does not guarantee what was given to her as gifts nor what she earned. He guarantees the value on each day of the claim, not the day of deception. And if he said to the market people: 'Deal with him,' then forbade one or two from dealing with him, then he was claimed, he does not guarantee those he forbade, because specifying the interdiction of trade is valid. And if a man entered the market with his slave and said: 'This is my slave, and I have given him permission to trade, and a debt has been incurred,' then he was found to be free, he is not a guarantor. And if he said: 'Deal with him,' he guarantees the lesser of the value or the debt. The difference: the command to deal does not separate from the obligation of debt, while permission separates. And if he said: 'This is my slave, so deal with him, and I have given him permission to trade,' and if he did not say 'I have given permission', he is a guarantor. And if he said: 'This is my mudabbar, I have given him permission to trade,' then a debt was incurred, and a man claimed him, the one who misled them guarantees nothing. And if he said: 'Deal with him in wheat,' if they dealt with him in it, he guarantees; if in every type, he guarantees. And if he said: 'I have given him permission to trade for specific people,' then others dealt with him, and he was found free or claimed by another, he guarantees only those he specifically commanded. If you say: It has preceded that the slave is sold for debt if he is given permission, even if he did not say 'deal with him,' and here he does not guarantee unless he said 'I have given permission and deal with him.' We say: This is daman ghurur, so for this he only guarantees those he specifically commanded to the amount of their share, because people differ in transactions. And if he said: 'Deal with him' and did not say he is his slave, he is not a guarantor and guarantees nothing to anyone. And if the one who said 'this is my slave' was a minor, a mukatab, or a mudabbar whose guarantee is not valid, he guarantees nothing. And in it: If he said: 'This is my son, and I have given him permission to trade, so deal with him,' and he was someone else's son, he is a guarantor because he made them hope that their debts become obligatory on the minor's liability and are recovered from his property due to his permission, and the matter turned out contrary. End.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of the ma'dhun slave, specifically addressing how permission is established and the liability for deception (ghurur). The commentator first states the general principle: the slave's trades are binding, and if the master admits to permission, the slave is sold for debts; otherwise not. Then he presents the istihsan basis: people deal with a slave who appears to have permission without requiring explicit proof at every transaction, due to necessity and widespread practice. The consensus of the Muslims (ijma) on this practice is a proof that custom can override textual requirements and qiyas. The second scenario covers when the slave trades without informing anyone; by istihsan, permission is presumed from the master's silence, as the slave's reason and religion prevent him from committing forbidden acts. The master cannot be forced to sell the slave's person for debts, as the body is his property; only the slave's earnings can be taken. Then the commentator cites al-Muhit to detail various ghurur cases. Ghurur is a form of tort liability: if a person through his statement or conduct misleads others into dealing with someone, causing loss, he is liable. The examples cover when the master makes public statements about the slave's status (e.g., 'this is my slave, I have permitted him'), whether he is liable for debts or value. The key distinction is between 'command to deal' (amr bil-mubay'a) and 'permission' (idhn): command implies the master's direct involvement and thus liability, while permission alone does not. Also, specifying a particular commodity (e.g., wheat) limits liability. If the master says nothing at all about the slave's identity, he is not liable. The lesson ends with the case of a person claiming 'this is my son' and giving permission, when the boy is actually another's; this is ghurur because he led others to believe the minor's property could satisfy debts.

Key Terms
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission granted by the master to the slave to engage in trade, which can be explicit or established by istihsan through silent acquiescence.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A method of juristic preference that overrides strict qiyas based on custom, necessity, or public interest; here, it validates the slave's trades without explicit permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the Hanafi school accept silent permission for the ma'dhun slave?
A) Because the slave's reason and religion prevent him from deceit.
B) Because of istihsan based on the consensus of Muslims and necessity.
C) Because the master is legally required to object.
D) Because the slave is considered the master's agent by default.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, silence coupled with custom can establish permission for trade, and this ruling is based on istihsan supported by consensus and necessity.

Ghurur Liability and the Difference Between Command and Permission
Commentary detailing ghurur liability cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where a master is liable for ghurur (deception) and cases where he is not, based on whether he issued a command to deal or merely granted permission, and identify the key factors that determine liability.

Core Idea
A master is liable for losses caused by his positive misrepresentation of a slave's status or permission, but not for mere silence or general permission.

The second half of this passage from al-Bahr al-Rai'q delves into the concept of daman al-ghurur (liability for deception) in transactions with a permitted slave. The commentator details numerous hypothetical cases to draw the line between when a master's statements about his slave make him liable for debts and when they do not. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating liability in trade relationships involving agents or dependents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for ghurur
The passage begins with examples from al-Muhit showing that if a master presents a slave-girl and says 'deal with her', then she is claimed, he guarantees her value and offspring. Note the timing of valuation: at claim day, not deception day.
Ghurur is established by statement or action that misleads others.
2
Distinguish between 'command to deal' and 'permission'
The key principle: commanding others to deal with someone (amr bil-mubaya'a) imposes liability for debts because it is inseparable from incurring debt; permission alone does not. The passage explains this difference.
The difference is crucial: if the master says 'deal with him' but not 'I gave permission', he is liable; if he says 'I gave permission' but not 'deal with him', he is not liable under ghurur, but the slave's trades are still valid.
3
Analyze cases of specific limitation
If the master forbids certain individuals from dealing with the slave, he is not liable to those he forbade if they still deal. Also, if the master specifies a commodity (e.g., wheat), liability is limited to that commodity.
Specificity limits liability.
4
Understand who is liable when the speaker is a minor or mukatab
If the person making the misrepresentation is a minor, mukatab, or mudabbar whose guarantee is invalid, he is not liable for ghurur.
Capacity for guarantee is relevant.
5
Synthesize the rule for 'this is my son' case
If someone says 'this is my son, I gave him permission, deal with him' and the boy is actually another's, he is liable because he led others to believe the minor's property could cover debts.
This is a clear case of ghurur.

Commentary

Key Terms
الغرور (al-ghurur): Deception or misrepresentation that induces another to enter a transaction, resulting in liability for the loss incurred by the deceived party.
الأمر بالمبايعة (al-amr bi al-mubaya'a): The command to deal with someone, which in Hanafi law implies the commander's guarantee of debts incurred.

According to the passage, in which case is the master not liable for ghurur?
A) He says 'This is my slave, deal with him' without mentioning permission.
B) He says 'I have given him permission' but does not command people to deal.
C) He says 'Deal with him in wheat' and they buy other goods.
D) He says 'This is my son, I have permitted him' and the son is actually another's.

Key Takeaway
The liability for ghurur in trade with a slave hinges on whether the master issued a command to deal (which implies guarantee) or merely granted permission (which does not).

Ruling When the Master Admits to Permission
Short commentary on the matn ruling for when the master admits permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master admits to giving permission to his slave and distinguish when his word is accepted versus when creditors' evidence prevails.

Core Idea
The master's admission binds him; his denial is temporary unless creditors prove permission with evidence.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentary in Bahr al-Raiq addresses a key scenario in slave trading: when the master is present and admits he gave the slave permission to trade, the slave is sold to satisfy debts. The ruling hinges on the principle of iqrar (admission), which is conclusive against the admitter. However, if the master denies permission and claims the slave was prohibited (mahjur 'alayhi), his word is initially accepted because the default is the master's authority; but the creditors can override this with clear evidence (bayyinah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The sale of a slave for debts incurred by his trading. What is the ruling when the master admits to permission? The sale is valid and binding.
2
Understand the Effect of Iqrar
The master's admission (iqrar) makes the debt apparent in his right. In Hanafi law, iqrar is a unilateral binding statement; the master cannot retract it once made.
This is based on the principle that iqrar is binding against the declarant.
3
Analyze the Exception: Master Denies
If the master says the slave was prohibited (mahjur 'alayhi), his word is accepted. Why? Because the default (zahir) is that the master has control; the burden of proof shifts to the creditors.
The phrase 'al-qawl qawluhu' indicates his statement is taken as true in the absence of contrary proof.
4
Apply the Role of Bayyinah
If the creditors bring legal evidence (bayyinah, e.g., two witnesses) that the master gave permission, this evidence overrides the master's denial. Bayyinah is described as 'confirmatory'—it establishes the truth like eyewitness certainty.
Bayyinah is equivalent to certainty ('iyan) in Hanafi thought, hence it prevails.
5
Summarize the Outcome
Master admits → slave sold. Master denies (no evidence) → not sold. Master denies (with creditors' evidence) → sold based on evidence.
This shows a balance between the master's rights and creditors' rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (فإن حضر وأقر بالإذن بيع وإلا فلا يعني إذا حضر المولى وأقر بإذنه بيع في الدين لظهور الدين في حقه بإقراره، وإن قال هو محجور عليه كان القول قوله لتمسكه بالظاهر إلا إذا أثبت الغرماء الإذن منه بالبينة كالثابت عياناً إذ هي مثبتة كاسمها
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If he is present and admits to permission, he is sold; otherwise not.) That is, if the master is present and admits to his permission, he is sold for the debt, due to the manifestation of the debt against him by his admission. And if he says he is interdicted, his word is accepted because he adheres to the apparent state, unless the creditors prove the permission from him by evidence, like that which is established by eyewitness, because it is confirmatory as its name implies.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This is a short ruling from the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) and its immediate commentary. The ruling: When the master is present and admits that he gave the slave permission to trade, the slave is sold to satisfy debts. This is because the master's admission (iqrar) makes the debt apparent in his right (i.e., he is liable to honor the permission). If the master denies and says he had actually prohibited the slave (he is a mahjur 'alayhi), his statement is taken as truthful because the default is that a master has control over his slave, and the burden is on the creditors to prove permission. However, if the creditors produce clear evidence (bayyinah) that the master had given permission, that evidence is decisive like eyewitness testimony. The phrase 'as its name implies' (kasma'uha) indicates that bayyinah literally manifests the truth.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession; here, the master's statement that he gave permission, which is legally binding against him.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females; here it overrides the master's denial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master is present in court and admits he gave permission to his slave. Later he changes his statement and says he actually prohibited the slave. How is this resolved?
A) The master's second statement is accepted because the default is prohibition.
B) The admission is binding; the slave is sold for debts regardless.
C) The creditors must bring proof; without it, the master's final statement stands.
D) The judge decides based on the slave's testimony.

Key Takeaway
The master's admission of permission is binding; only clear evidence from creditors can override his denial.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
